Distinction between Complete Definition and Its Parts
Author defines the distinction between two types of answers to 'what is it?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'al-maqul fi jawab ma huwa' (complete definition) and 'al-maqul bi tariq ma huwa' (individual essential part) and identify each in a definition from an Islamic science.

Core Idea
The complete essence answer is the whole; each essential part is a piece mentioned directly.

If someone asks you 'What is prayer?', the complete answer includes all essential parts: intention, takbir, recitation, ruku', sujud, etc. But if you only say 'intention', that is part of the answer. The logicians have precise terms for this distinction. Let's break down the passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key expressions
In the Arabic text, locate 'المقول في جواب ما هو' (al-maqul fi jawab ma huwa) and 'المقول بطريق ما هو' (al-maqul bi tariq ma huwa). These are the two main categories being distinguished.
2
Understand the criterion: completeness vs. correspondence
The text explains that the first is the complete answer covering all essential parts of the essence (تمام الماهية). The second is each individual part if mentioned by direct correspondence (بالمطابقة), i.e., just as it exists in the essence.
The phrase 'لو كان مذكورا بالمطابقة' indicates that the part is mentioned exactly as it is a part.
3
Apply to a simple Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'human' in falsafa: 'rational animal'. The complete definition (maqul fi jawab ma huwa) is 'rational animal'. The genus 'animal' alone, or the differentia 'rational' alone, each is maqul bi tariq ma huwa if mentioned by itself.
This shows how the complete answer includes both parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المقول في جواب ما هو والمقول بطريق ما هو: أن المقول في جواب ما هو هو السؤال الطالب تمام الماهية، فلا يكون جوابه إلا بذكر جميع أجزائها. تمام هذا الجواب هو المقول جواب ما هو، وكل واحد من أجزائه هو المقول في طريق ما هو لو كان مذكورا بالمطابقة. والمقول في جواب ما هو على أقسام: منها المقول في جواب ما هو تحت الشركة، وهو تمام الجزء المشترك بين الأمور المختلفة بالماهية. فإن كان ذلك الجزء المشترك بين كل اثنين منها هو بعينه تمام القدر المشترك بين الكل، فكلما كانت الأنواع أكثر تباعدا في الجنس كان الجواب واحدا. وإن كان مختلفا، فكلما كانت الأنواع أكثر تباعدا كان الجواب أكثر.
The distinction between what is said in answer to 'what is it?' and what is said by way of 'what is it?': that what is said in answer to 'what is it?' is the question demanding the complete essence, so its answer is not but by mentioning all its parts. The complete of this answer is what is said in answer to 'what is it?', and every one of its parts is what is said by way of 'what is it?' if it is mentioned by way of correspondence. And what is said in answer to 'what is it?' has divisions: among them is what is said in answer to 'what is it?' under the commonality, which is the complete common part between things differing in essence. If that common part between every two of them is exactly the complete common measure between all, then however many the species are more diverse in genus, the answer is one. And if it is different, then whenever the species are more diverse, the answer is more.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage where the author distinguishes between two types of expressions related to the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa). The first is 'maqul fi jawab ma huwa' (what is said in answer to 'what is it?'), which is the complete answer that includes all essential parts of a thing's essence. The second is 'maqul bi tariq ma huwa' (what is said by way of 'what is it?'), which refers to each individual essential part if it is mentioned directly (bi al-mutabaqa). The author then divides 'maqul fi jawab ma huwa' into categories. One category is 'what is said in answer to 'what is it?' under commonality', i.e., the genus that is the common part among different species. The author then discusses whether this common part is the same for all species or differs. If the common part between any two species is identical to the common part among all species, then regardless of the number of species, the answer (the genus) remains one. If the common part differs, then greater diversity of species leads to a more diverse answer (i.e., multiple genera). This is foundational for understanding the theory of definition (hadd) and the five universals, especially genus and species. The discussion implicitly uses examples from Islamic sciences, such as 'animal' as the genus for 'human', 'horse', etc., and how they share a common essence in animality.

Key Terms
المقول في جواب ما هو
المقول بطريق ما هو
تمام الماهية
الجزء المشترك
المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the study of definition, what is the difference between 'maqul fi jawab ma huwa' and 'maqul bi tariq ma huwa'?
A) The former is a part of the latter.
B) The former is the complete answer including all essential parts; the latter is each part mentioned directly.
C) The former is the genus; the latter is the differentia.
D) Both are identical.

Key Takeaway
The complete definition includes all essential parts; each part mentioned by correspondence is a partial answer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition of 'iman' in Ash'ari theology: 'Iman is affirmation in the heart, utterance with the tongue, and action with the limbs.' Which part of this definition is maqul fi jawab ma huwa and which parts are maqul bi tariq ma huwa? Explain.
?
In the definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh: 'Qiyas is the application of the ruling of an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause.' Identify the complete definition and one of its essential parts.

Essence and Its Parts: Definition and Priority
Author explains the requirements for a complete definition of essence and debates the priority of its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a complete definition of an essence and explain why the parts are not prior to the whole by the essence itself.

Core Idea
An essence is defined by its parts, but the parts are not prior to the whole in essence; priority is a matter of dependence.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas tackles two intertwined questions: what constitutes a complete answer to 'what is it?' and how to conceive of the relationship between an essence and its parts. Understanding these points is crucial for correct definition and for avoiding metaphysical confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'mahiyyah' (essence) in the Arabic. Note that the author is discussing the universal essence and its definition.
2
Extract the common part and differentia
The common part (al-juz' al-mushtarak) is the genus shared by species. The differentia distinguishes species within that genus. In a complete definition, both must be mentioned.
If the genus is one and the same for all species, a single definition suffices; otherwise, the definition varies.
3
Check definition completeness
A complete definition (hadd) includes all essential parts. The author states that the answer to 'ma huwa' must mention all parts of the essence. An incomplete definition (rasm) would lack something.
4
Analyze the priority claim
The author considers whether parts are prior to the whole. He rejects priority by essence (bi nafs al-mahiyyah) because the essence in itself is not subject to priority or posteriority.
5
Reconstruct the argument against priority by essence
If parts were prior by essence, then the essence would be prior to itself, which is impossible. This is an indirect proof (burhan al-khalf).
The author also discusses priority in existence, but clarifies that existence is not prior to essence.

Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a dense discussion on the nature of mahiyyah (essence), its parts, and the concept of priority (taqaddum) of parts over the whole. It begins by addressing the answer to the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa) for a universal essence. The author states that a complete answer requires mentioning all parts of the essence. The answer that indicates the essence is called essential (dhati). He then distinguishes between the common part (al-juz' al-mushtarak), which is the genus shared by species differing in essence, and the differentia. The author argues that if the common part is one genus, then regardless of the number of species, the answer is one, because the common element between any two is the same for all. If not, then as species become more distant in genus, the answer varies. The text then shifts to the priority of parts. If mahiyyah is composite, its parts are prior to it in some way. The author rejects the idea that parts are prior by essence (bi nafs al-mahiyyah) because mahiyyah considered in itself is not subject to priority or posteriority. Priority in existence (bi al-wujud) is also problematic because existence is not prior to mahiyyah; rather, mahiyyah is prior in essence. The author responds to an objection (naqd) about the priority of parts, clarifying that the priority meant is not by essence but by something else, likely by causality or essential dependence. The discussion is technical, aiming to clarify the relationship between essence, its parts, and existence, common in philosophical discourse.

الجواب
الجزء المشترك

Checkpoint
According to the text, why cannot the priority of a part over the whole be by the essence itself?
A) Because the part is identical to the whole.
B) Because the essence considered in itself is not subject to priority or posteriority.
C) Because existence is prior to the essence.
D) Because priority requires a temporal relation.

Key Takeaway
A complete definition of an essence requires mentioning all its parts (genus and differentia), and parts are not prior to the whole by the essence itself, but by something else like causal dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of wudu as 'washing the face and hands and wiping the head and feet'. Identify its genus (act of purification) and differentia (specific body parts). Assess whether this is a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm).
?
In a kalam argument, if one claims that the world is temporally originated (hadith) because it changes, and everything that changes is temporally originated, extract the common part and differentia in the definition of 'hadith' as used by mutakallimun.

Priority of Parts and Critique of Detailed Knowledge
Author argues parts are prior by existence and critiques detailed knowledge requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's argument for the priority of parts by existence and critique the claim that parts must be known in detail before the whole.

Core Idea
Priority belongs to existence, not quiddity itself; detailed conceptual knowledge is not necessary.

In this passage, the author engages with two intertwined issues: the metaphysical priority of parts relative to a composite quiddity, and the epistemological requirement for knowing those parts. He argues that priority cannot be intrinsic to quiddity—since quiddity as such is neutral—but must be through existence. He then targets the view that before knowing a composite, one must know its parts in full detail, showing that this leads to an infinite regress or contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims under critique
The author first addresses the metaphysical claim that parts cannot be prior to the whole in existence. Then he critiques the epistemological claim that parts of a composite quiddity must be known in full detail before the whole can be conceived. Locate these two claims in the Arabic passage.
The transition occurs around 'ثم قال' indicating a shift to the epistemological point.
2
Analyze the argument for priority by existence
Notice how the author rejects that priority can be by the quiddity itself (since quiddity is neutral to priority). He asserts that priority is by existence, giving the example of one part's existence necessitating the nonexistence of another. Trace the logical move from 'فإن عنيت' to 'فلم لا نجوز التقدم بالوجود'.
3
Reconstruct the objection about detailed knowledge
The objector claims that for a composite quiddity to be known, its parts must be known in detail (على التفصيل). Identify the author's response: if parts are known individually, they must be distinguished; if not known, the whole cannot be conceived. The author provides counterexamples from natural (animal body) and artificial (bed) divisions.
4
Evaluate the author's critique
Assess whether the author successfully refutes the requirement for detailed knowledge. Consider whether the examples of natural and artificial parts convincingly show that we conceive wholes without detailing parts. Reflect on the logical consequence (infinite regress) that the author implies.
The critique is based on our immediate conception of composites like 'horse' or 'bed' without enumerating every limb or component.

متع عقلنا ما هيتين مخلفين عن المنتج ان لا نعقل امتياز احدهما عن الآخر مال علينا بها و ان لم يكن معلومًا مع إنا نعلى الذات فذلك يقلى مين ان نصور الخلق متوقف عانصو راجراته مع إن ذلك معلوم بالضرورة تالف الماهية عن جزاها قد تكون تالفا طبيعيا كالف بدن الحيوان عن الاعضاء و قد يكون صناعيا كالف السرير عن اجران
It has no individual unit at all, so when they combine no single quiddity results, and those parts do not [؟]... the priority of the part over the whole cannot be by the quiddity itself... but by the reality of existence... and the consideration of the parts of quiddity with it is a consideration that obtains before existence. We respond to the objection that it is refuted... And the second: that the priority of the quiddity over its parts by the quiddity itself is impossible... If you mean that the reality itself has no priority, then it is so; if you mean something else, it is denied... The Quiddity, inasmuch as it is itself, is distinct from what is added to it... Why do we not allow priority by existence? The antecedent of priority by existence is not conceded because if something is essential and the existence of one necessitates the nonexistence of the other, then that relation is necessary... Then he said: The parts of the composite quiddity, if they are known, must be distinguished in the mind... if they are not known while we know the whole, then the conception of the whole depends on the conception of its parts, which is known necessarily. The division of quiddity into its parts may be natural, like the division of the animal body into limbs, or artificial, like the division of the bed into its parts.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage engages in a critical discussion about the priority of parts relative to a composite quiddity (mahiyya murakkaba). The author addresses the claim that parts cannot be prior to the whole in existence. He argues that priority cannot be in the quiddity itself because quiddity as such is neutral to priority and posteriority. Instead, priority is in existence. He then responds to an objection that priority of parts is impossible, asserting that if the existence of one part necessitates the nonexistence of another, then the parts are prior. The passage then transitions to an epistemological point: Sheikh al-Ishraq (Suhrawardi?) argued that parts must be known in detail before the composite. The author critiques this, saying that if parts are known individually, they must be distinguished; if not known, it is impossible to conceive the whole without conceiving the parts—yet we know the whole necessarily. Thus, the parts need not be known in detail conceptually prior to the whole. The author concludes by distinguishing natural and artificial decomposition of quiddity into parts.

Key Terms
الماهية
التقدم بالوجود
الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The quiddity itself is prior by nature
B) The existence of the parts is prior
C) The parts are conceptually prior
D) The parts are known first in the mind

Key Takeaway
Priority of parts is by existence, not quiddity, and detailed conceptual knowledge of parts is not required for conceiving the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Different Definitions of Dhati (Essential) in Logic
Author enumerates and critiques various definitions of dhati in the logical tradition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the various definitions of dhati proposed by logicians and identify the definition preferred by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun).

Core Idea
The verifiers restrict dhati to what is part of the quiddity, excluding mere necessary concomitants.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents multiple interpretations of the term 'dhati' (essential) from the logical tradition. Some definitions are too broad, including any necessary concomitant; others are too vague. The verifiers settle on a precise definition: dhati is that which constitutes a part of the quiddity. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term under dispute is 'dhati' (الذاتي). Recognize that it refers to what is essential or intrinsic to a thing's quiddity (mahiyya). In logic, dhati is crucial for constructing correct definitions (hadd).
Dhati is often contrasted with 'aradi' (accidental).
2
List all proposed definitions
Write down the five definitions mentioned: (1) that without which the essence cannot exist; (2) that which the mind cannot abstract from the quiddity; (3) that which cannot be separated from the quiddity; (4) that which the mind cannot ignore when aware of the subject; (5) that which is a part of the quiddity.
The fourth definition is criticized for being too restrictive.
3
Classify definitions by inclusivity
Definitions 1-4 are broad: they include any necessary concomitant, even if not a constituent of the essence. Definition 5 is narrow: it restricts dhati to what is genuinely part of the quiddity. This distinction is the crux of the dispute.
The broader definitions risk conflating essential and accidental properties.
4
Identify the verifiers' position
The author states that the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) adopt definition 5: dhati is what is a part of the quiddity. Their reasoning is that necessary concomitants, though inseparable, are not part of the thing's essence. This is the most precise definition.
Al-Razi himself aligns with the verifiers.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
Use this classification to evaluate definitions in fiqh, usul, or kalam. For example, in defining 'iman' (faith), is 'belief in the heart' essential (dhati) while 'action of the limbs' is a necessary concomitant? This depends on the definition of dhati.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في تفسير الذاتي فمنهم من فسره بأنه الذي يمتنع خلق الذات عنه ويندرج فيه جميع لوازم الوجود والماهية ومنهم من فسره بأخصر منه وهو الذي يمتنع خلوص الذهن في ماهيته عنه ومنهم من فسره بأخصر من وموالذي يمتنع ملاة وته عن الماهية ومنهم من جعله الأخص وسواه الذي تكون بين الثبور اتحالة والبين الشوق لله هو ما تكون محبت منع شعود الذهن الشر الامع شعوره بذلك الوصف وانا كان الخصرين الثالث لانه من الجانو ان تكون القصف بحيث لكن الغفلة عنه مع الشعود بالموضو الا انه من حمل الشعود سوق الموصوف فانه لا تمكن الذهن من ان الله عنه ومنهم من جعله إحص وهو الذي يكون جزء الماهية وهم المحققون والنزاع في منها
They differed in the interpretation of 'dhati' (essential). Among them is one who interpreted it as that which prevents the creation of the essence from it, and includes all necessary concomitants of existence and quiddity. Among them is one who interpreted it more concisely: that which prevents the mind from separating it in its quiddity. Among them is one who interpreted it even more concisely: that which prevents separation from the quiddity. Among them is one who made it the most specific: what is between the two [prior] is impossibility and the separation is that which prevents the mind's attention from it while being aware of the subject, except that it is from the burden of awareness of the described, for the mind cannot ignore it. Among them is one who made it the most specific: what is a part of the quiddity, and they are the verifiers, and the dispute is about which.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents different definitions of the term 'dhati' (essential) as used in logic and metaphysics. The author outlines several views. The first definition: dhati is that without which the essence cannot exist, encompassing all necessary concomitants of existence and quiddity. The second, more concise: dhati is that which the mind cannot abstract from the quiddity. The third, even more concise: dhati is that which cannot be separated from the quiddity at all. The fourth, most specific: dhati is that which the mind cannot be oblivious to when aware of the subject, but this is criticized because awareness of the subject implies the mind cannot ignore it. The final definition, which the author favors as held by the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), is that dhati is what is a part (juz') of the quiddity. The passage ends by noting that the dispute is about which definition is correct.

الجزء
الماهية

D) That which the mind cannot be oblivious to

Key Takeaway
The verifiers define dhati as what is part of the quiddity, thereby excluding necessary concomitants that are not constitutive of the essence.

The Principle that Parts of Quiddity Admit No Intensity
Al-Razi discusses the nature of parts of quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a part of quiddity (جزء الماهية) cannot vary in intensity or weakness using al-Razi's argument.

Core Idea
A part of quiddity is either present or absent — it cannot be weakened.

Al-Razi argues that the components of an essence are not subject to degrees. If a part were weakened, the essence would either remain with a superfluous extra element or cease to exist entirely. This subtle point is crucial for understanding why definitions are precise and not subject to variation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Al-Razi states that a part of quiddity does not accept intensity or weakness (لا يقبل الأشد والأضعف).
2
Analyze the two scenarios
If the part is weakened, either the quiddity remains (then the supposed increase becomes an extra that fulfills its realization) or the quiddity does not remain (then the weakness is simply non-existence).
3
Apply to genera
The parts of genera are finite; otherwise they would not be intelligible. This reinforces that essential parts are fixed, not variable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفاسير المتعلقة باللفظ وهو غير لائق بالمباحث الحقيقية. وهو أيضا متعدد، لأن الذاتي معنى متحد في اللغة المنتسب إلى الذات من حيث هو داخل أو خارج، لازم أو مفارق. وأصل المعنى وهو غير ممكن، لأن من فسر لفظه لا يمكن أن يقال له أصبت أو أخطأت. نعم الذي يبحث لتمييز هذه المعاني بعضها عن بعض، وإن كان التركيب الذاتي والعارض لها بعد، والفرق بينهما أولى والاحتراز عن الغلط الواقع بسبب الاشتراك. جزء الماهية لا يقبل الأشد والأضعف، لأن الماهية بعد ضعف ذلك الجزء إن بقي كان الزائد خبرة تحققها، وإن لم يبق كان الضعف عدما. وكذا القول في الأجزاء إن بيعت الماهية كانت الزيادة حشوا إن كان ذلك كذلك. أجزاء الأجناس متناهية وإلا لم تكن معقولة. لفظ الذاتي قد يقال على معان متعلقة بالحمل على ما لا يكون كذلك. فالأول يقال بالاشتراك على معان: يقال محمول بالذات إذا كان الموضوع مستحقا لمحموليته كقولنا الإنسان أبيض، وبالعرض كقولنا الأبيض. وعلى الوصف الحاصل للشيء حقيقة سواء كان عن طبيعة أو بقاسر لوجه فيه كقولنا الضاحك من كل بالذات وإن كان ذلك بالقوة. والمحمول بالعرض كقولنا للمساكن في السفينة إنه متحرك. وعند الحمل على الأخص كالحيوان على الإنسان فهو قريب من الأول. وعلى ما لا تكون بينه وبين موضوعه واسطة كقولنا سطح أبيض، وبالعرض على ما يكون هناك واسطة. والمحمول بالذات ما كان وجوده على الموضوع ليس بعرض خارجي بل لذاته، كالحجم المتحرك إلى أسفل. والمحمول بالعرض كالجو المتحرك إلى فوق. وعلى الوصف الذي لحق الماهية لأمر عارض كقولنا الحيوان متحرك. وهو المذكور في كتاب البرهان.
The interpretations related to wording are not suitable for real investigations. And it is also multiple, because the essential meaning is one in language pertaining to the essence in terms of being inside or outside, necessary or separable. And the original meaning is impossible, because one who interprets its wording cannot be told 'you are correct' or 'you are wrong'. Rather, what is researched is to distinguish these meanings from each other, even if the essential composition and accidental are later, and the difference between them is more important and avoidance of error occurring due to ambiguity. A part of quiddity does not accept intensity and weakness, because the quiddity after the weakness of that part: if it remains, the increase is an extra that fulfills its realization, and if it does not remain, the weakness is non-existence. And similarly for the parts: if the quiddity is sold, the increase is superfluous if that is so. The parts of genera are finite, otherwise they would not be intelligible. The term 'essential' may be said for meanings related to predication on what is not like that. So the first is said by homonymy for meanings: it is said 'predicated essentially' if the subject is deserving of its predication, like our saying 'man is white', and accidentally like our saying 'the white one'. And upon the description that belongs to the thing truly, whether from nature or by a separator in it, like our saying 'the one who laughs from every [person] essentially' even if that is potential. And accidental predication is like our saying of the occupants of the ship 'they are moving'. And upon the predication of the more specific on the more general, like 'animal' on 'man', it is close to the first. And upon what has no intermediary between it and its subject, like our saying 'a white surface', and accidental is when there is an intermediary. And essentially predicated is that whose existence on the subject is not by an external accident but essentially, like a volume moving downward. And accidentally predicated is like air moving upward. And upon the description that attaches to the quiddity due to an accidental matter, like our saying 'an animal is moving'. And it is mentioned in the Book of Demonstration.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the multiple meanings of 'essential' (ذاتي) and clarifies the distinction between essential and accidental predication. The author begins by noting that interpretations based solely on wording are unsuitable for real investigations, as the term 'essential' has multiple senses. He emphasizes that the part of quiddity (جزء الماهية) cannot vary in intensity because if the part is weakened, either the quiddity remains intact (making the increase superfluous) or the part disappears (making the weakness nonexistence). He then lists various senses of 'predication by essence' (بالحمل الذاتي): when the predicate is constitutive of the subject, when the subject is inherently deserving of the predicate (e.g., 'man is white' in the sense of essential whiteness), and when no intermediary exists (e.g., 'surface is white'). Accidental predication (بالحمل العرضي) occurs when an intermediary is present, e.g., 'the ship occupant is moving' due to the ship's motion, or 'air moving upward' against its natural tendency. The passage prepares the ground for the subsequent classification of concomitants.

Key Terms
جزء الماهية
الذاتي
الأشد والأضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because quiddity is indivisible
B) Because if weakened, either the quiddity remains with a superfluous increase or it ceases to exist
C) Because parts of genera are infinite
D) Because intensity only applies to accidents

Key Takeaway
A part of quiddity is all-or-nothing; it cannot vary in degree without destroying the essence.

The Multiple Senses of Essential and Accidental Predication
Al-Razi enumerates senses of essential and accidental predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential predication from accidental predication using the criterion of the presence or absence of an intermediary (wasita).

Core Idea
Essential predication is direct; accidental predication always involves an intermediary.

Al-Razi now lists the various homonymous uses of 'essential' in predication, providing concrete examples. The key distinction is the presence or absence of an intermediary between subject and predicate. Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing how predicates attach to subjects in logical and theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonymy of 'essential'
Al-Razi notes that the term 'essential' (al-dhati) is used homonymously (bi al-ishtirak) for several meanings related to predication.
This alerts us to avoid ambiguity.
2
First essential sense: inherent deserving
When the subject is inherently deserving of the predicate's predication, e.g., 'man is white' — man is capable of whiteness, so whiteness is essential in this sense.
This is not constitutive but still essential.
3
Second essential sense: no intermediary
When there is no intermediary between subject and predicate, e.g., 'a surface is white' — whiteness is directly on the surface.
The absence of a wasita is the key indicator.
4
Accidental predication via intermediary
When an intermediary is present, e.g., 'the ship occupant is moving' — movement is due to the ship, not the occupant's essence.
The movement is accidental because it comes through the ship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

8 min
Sixfold Classification of Concomitants
Author enumerates six types of concomitants based on essential/accidental and generality/specificity/equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any given concomitant attribute as essential or accidental and as more general, equal, or more specific, using examples from Kalam.

Core Idea
Concomitants are classified by two axes: essential vs accidental and generality vs specificity vs equality.

In Islamic logic, attributes that accompany a subject (concomitants) vary in their relation to the subject. They may belong to its essence or to something else, and they may be broader, narrower, or coextensive. This lesson unpacks the sixfold classification, a precise analytical tool for understanding predicates in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions
Note the two axes: essential (لذاته) vs accidental (لغيره) and the three scope relations: more general (أعم), equal (مساوٍ), more specific (أخص). These give 2×3=6 types.
2
Examine examples for essential concomitants
From the passage: essential-general: individuality for three (individuality is broader than three); essential-equal: 'the possible is needful' (need is coextensive with possibility); essential-specific: 'the existent with the one and the many' (each mode is a narrower attribute).
3
Examine examples for accidental concomitants
Accidental-equal: 'bodies by their container' (the attribute equals the subject); accidental-specific: 'laughter by the power of wonder' (laughter is specific to humans, though the cause is equal to reason).
The passage does not provide an example for accidental-general; it may be inferred as an attribute broader than the subject but not essential.
4
Apply the classification to a Kalam example
Consider: 'The necessary being exists by itself.' Here existence is an essential equal concomitant, since existence is equal to necessary being and belongs to its essence. This classification helps distinguish essential from accidental attributes of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إما أن تكون الجفالة لذاته أو لغيره. وعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون ذلك اللاحق أعم أو أخص أو مساويًا. فهذه أقسام ستة. فالذي يلحقه لذاته ويكون أعم بواسطة كالفردية للثلاثة. والذى يلحقه لذاته ويكون مساويًا كقولنا الممكن محتاج لذاته. والذي يلحقه لذاته ويكون أخص وذلك إذا افتقر إلى الإنصاف بأحد الأمرين لا بعينه وكل واحد منها أخص كالموجود مع الواحد والكثير. والآخر الذي يلحقه الوصف مساوٍ له وهو من اللاحق الذي لحق لوصف حاوٍ له وهو مأخذ الأجسام بواسطة حاويها كالحلول على الإنسان بواسطة التحجّي. واللاحق الذي لحقه صوراف حاوٍ له وهو أخص كفعل الضحك بواسطة قوة التعجب المساوية لقوة النطق. وأما اللذان لا تعلقات بالحمل المذكور فالقائم بذاته أنه موجود بذاته، والقائم بغيره أنه ليس بذاته. يقال بذاته لله الذي هو سيد الأوضاع المساوية للأجسام وامتناع التداخل أعم من الحمام المحصول المعقول المجردة والنفوس.
Either the attribute is for its essence or for another. And in both cases, that concomitant is either more general, more specific, or equal. So these are six divisions. That which attaches to it essentially and is more general by an intermediary, like individuality for three. And that which attaches to it essentially and is equal, like our saying 'the possible is needful essentially'. And that which attaches to it essentially and is more specific, and that occurs when it requires the prudence of one of two matters not in itself, and each of them is more specific, like the existent with the one and the many. And the other which the description attaches to it is equal to it, and it is of the concomitant that attached to a description containing it, like the taking of bodies by means of their container, like solution in man by means of art. And the concomitant that attached to it is a form containing it, and it is more specific, like the act of laughter by means of the power of wonder equal to the power of speech. And as for the two that have no relation to the mentioned predication: the self-subsistent is that it is existent by itself, and the subsistent by another is that it is not by itself. It is said 'by itself' of God, who is the master of the positions equal to bodies, and the impossibility of interpenetration is more general than the acquired intellect, abstract intelligibles, and souls.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of six types of concomitants (اللواحق). The author first divides them into essential (لذاته) and accidental (لغيره). Within each, they are further categorized as more general (أعم), more specific (أخص), or equal (مساوٍ). Examples: (1) Essential-general: individuality for three (since three is a number, individuality applies to it and to others). (2) Essential-equal: 'the possible is essentially needful' (need is coextensive with possibility). (3) Essential-specific: 'existent' with respect to 'one' and 'many' (each is a specific mode of existence). (4) Accidental-equal: e.g., bodies are taken by their container; solution in man by means of art (the attribute equals the subject). (5) Accidental-specific: e.g., laughter by the power of wonder (laughter is specific to humans, but the cause is equal to reason). (6) The last two are unrelated to predication: the self-subsistent (God) exists by Himself, and what subsists by another is not self-subsistent. The passage ends with a remark that the impossibility of interpenetration is more general than acquired intellect, abstract intelligibles, and souls.

External Necessary Concomitant: Definition, Objections, and Resolution
Author defines external necessary concomitant and examines objections to its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of external necessary concomitant and explain the critique regarding the nature of the concomitance relation.

Core Idea
The external necessary concomitant must be permanent, but its relation eludes categorization; it is self-evident.

In this passage, Shaykh Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defines the external necessary concomitant (al-lāzim al-khārijī) as that which accompanies the essence without being part of it. He then anticipates two objections: first, that separable concomitants would also fit if permanence is not required; second, that even with permanence, accidental coincidental coexistence would be a counterexample. The deeper problem concerns the ontological status of the relation (luzūm) itself. Al-Razi argues that the relation cannot be non-existent, nor identical to the relata, nor a third entity without infinite regress. His final resolution is that such relations are among the primary self-evident truths (awwaliyyāt) and require no further proof. This lesson unpacks these layers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the official definition
Find in the Arabic where al-Shaykh defines the external necessary concomitant. What are the two conditions: accompaniment and not being part of the essence?
The Arabic is corrupt but the translation states: 'that which accompanies the essence and is not part of it'.
2
Analyze the first objection (al-mufāriq)
The definition is challenged by separable concomitants. How does al-Razi respond? By adding the condition of permanence (dawām) to 'accompaniment'.
Without permanence, anything that sometimes accompanies would count, so permanence is required.
3
Examine the second objection (accidental concomitance)
Even with permanence, things that permanently coexist by coincidence (ittifāq) would be counterexamples. What is al-Razi's reply? The text is corrupt here; the commentary suggests it is a known counterexample.
The author seems to acknowledge the objection but does not fully resolve it here.
4
Unpack the critique of the relation itself
The passage then questions the nature of the relation (luzūm). Why can't it be non-existent? Why can't it be identical to the relata? Why can't it be a third thing? What does al-Razi conclude? That the relation is among primary self-evident truths.
Each alternative leads to absurdity, so the relation is accepted as primitive.

On the investigation of external necessary concomitants: and it is [??] and the definition of external necessary concomitant: The Shaykh said: It is that which accompanies the essence and is not part of it. It is refuted by the separable [concomitant] unless by 'accompaniment' is meant its permanence. Then it is refuted by things that each permanently accompany the other by way of coincidence. Our minds: whenever a human is rational, a donkey is [??] except that [??] on the health of the single [??]. But it is not known necessarily. However, one may say: if something is a necessary concomitant, then the relation of concomitance is either non-existent, which is impossible because there is no difference between a non-existent concomitance and non-existence of [??]; or existent, and its referent is either to the essence of the subject and the necessary concomitant, which is impossible because we can conceive them while heedless of that relation, and because the relation is between two things so it is distinct from them; or to something else, which is impossible because if it were a necessary concomitant, then its concomitance would require another relation and an infinite regress would follow. And it is possible [??] because it is in estimation [??] so it is not the necessary concomitant because this is a contradiction. And if the relation were realized, then the non-existence of the subject would necessitate the non-existence of the necessary concomitant, so the relation would be between the two [mental] concepts, and whatever is a description of non-existence cannot be a [??]. The answer: it is among the primary self-evident truths, so it does not require proof.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines the external necessary concomitant (al-lāzim al-khārijī) as that which always accompanies the essence (dhāt) without being part of it. The author then presents a critique: the definition is refuted by separable concomitants (al-mufāriq) unless 'accompaniment' means permanent concurrence. Even then, things that permanently accompany each other by accident (ittifāq) constitute a counterexample. The passage then delves into the nature of the relation (luzūm) itself: it cannot be non-existent (because that would collapse into no relation), and if existent, it cannot be identical to the subject or the concomitant (since we conceive them without it), nor can it be a third thing (because that would require another relation and infinite regress). The response is that the relation is among the primary self-evident truths (awwaliyyāt), so it does not require a separate proof. The discussion reflects the logical tradition's careful analysis of concomitance and its epistemological status.

Can One Species Have Two Differentiae? The Debate
Author presents objections against multiple differentiae for one species and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against multiple differentiae for a single species and analyze the author's refutation using the principle that one effect cannot have two independent causes.

Core Idea
A species cannot have two independent differentiae because a single effect cannot have two independent causes.

In the Islamic logical tradition, the differentia (fasl) is the constitutive attribute that defines a species. A key question arises: can a species have two differentiae? Some argue that if multiple differentiae were possible, the same species would be explained by two causes—an impossibility. This lesson walks through the objection and the author's subtle refutation, distinguishing between real and conceptual differentiae and analyzing causal priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim against multiple differentiae: if a species had two differentiae, it would have two causes for its existence, which is impossible (one effect cannot have two independent causes).
The objector assumes each differentia is a real cause.
2
Examine the first answer
The author responds that a differentia could be a mere non-existence (e.g., 'not-animal'), which is a conceptual matter, not a real attribute. Such a differentia does not act as a real cause, so the objection fails.
This answer addresses cases where the differentia is a privation.
3
Examine the second answer
The author argues that in a single rank (one level of classification), the differentia must be one, because it is the perfection of the distinguishing part, and a single perfection cannot be multiple.
This is a conceptual argument about the nature of perfection.
4
Analyze the composite differentia scenario
The objector suggests that two partial differentiae might together produce the species. The author replies that the composite is truly one differentia, and if the parts are not independent, they depend on the species, leading to circular priority between species and parts.
This refutation shows that partial differentiae cannot combine into a genuine single reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والا لزم تعليل الشي الواحد لعلتي[؟] تكون. والجواب عن الأول: أن كون العدم فصلا إنما هو أمر اعتبادي لا خارجي فلا تمنع أن يكون مقوما من هذا الوجه. وعن الثاني: أن التعيين لم يصدر إلا بأحدهما والالتحكم المحاد[؟] الذي ذكر فوات الفصل في الدرجة الواحدة لا يكون إلا واحدا لأن الفصل كمال الجزء المميز وذلك لا يكون إلا واحدا وإلا لم يكن كمالا. واحتج الشيخ بأن الفصل علة لوجود الحصة والمعلول الواحد بالشخص لا يكون عليه علتان مختلفتان. فإن جاز وجود شيئين يكون كل واحد منها ممتز[؟] لكن لا يكون واحد منها مستقلا بالتحصيل بل المستقل لذلك المجموعة، لانا نقول: فالمجموع بالحقيقة فصل واحد وكل واحد منها جزوه. وأيضا فكل واحد من الجزئين إذا لم تكن مقومة للحصة وجب أن تكون منفوحة[؟] بها وإلا لكان القصر[؟] والحصة يستغني كل واحد منهما عن الآخر. وقد يتنا[؟] أن مثل هذين لا تكون من الاجتماعها حقيقة واحدة. وإذا كان كذلك كانت الحصة سابقة عليهما وهما سابقان على المجموع سبق البسيط على المراتب.
Otherwise it would necessitate explaining one thing by two causes. The answer to the first: that the non-existence being a differentia is a relational matter, not real, so it does not prevent being constitutive from this aspect. And to the second: that the specification was not made except by one of them, and the single rank of the differentia, as mentioned, is only one because the differentia is the perfection of the distinguishing part, and that is only one, otherwise it would not be a perfection. The Master argued that the differentia is the cause of the existence of the species, and one individual effect cannot have two different causes. If it is possible for two things to exist each of which is [partial] but neither is independent in producing, rather the independent is the composite, then we say: the composite is in reality one differentia and each of them is part of it. Also, each of the two parts, if it is not constitutive of the species, must be dependent on it; otherwise the part and the species would each be independent of the other. And it is known that such two [parts] do not form one reality from their combination. And if so, the species is prior to them, and they are prior to the composite, with the priority of the simple over the composite.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and its answer concerning whether a single species can be constituted by two differentiae. The objector claims that if a species had two differentiae, that would entail that one thing (the species) has two causes, which is impossible. The author responds with two points. First, he addresses the idea that a differentia could be a non-existence (like 'not-animal'?); he says that non-existence as a differentia is merely a conceptual consideration, not a real external attribute, so it does not prevent being constitutive in that aspect. Second, he argues that the specific difference in a single rank (i.e., in one level of classification) is only one, because the differentia is the perfection of the distinct part, and a single perfection cannot be multiple. Then he mentions an argument from the Master (likely Ibn Sina): the differentia is a cause of the species, and a single effect cannot have two different causes. The objector then raises a scenario: what if two things together produce the species, but neither is independent? The author replies that in reality the composite is one differentia, and each part is just a component. Furthermore, if neither part is constitutive alone, they must be dependent on the species, but then the species would be prior to them, and they are prior to the composite—a circularity that shows such parts cannot combine to form a single true reality. Thus, the species cannot have two independent differentiae.

A species cannot have two independent differentiae because a single effect cannot have two independent causes, and any attempt to combine partial differentiae leads to circularity or conceptual confusion.

Critique of Using Differentia as First Cause
Author critiques the Shaykh's view that the final differentia is the first cause and the highest genus is the last effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in using the claim that the final differentia is the first cause to prove the finitude of ascending genera.

Core Idea
A proof of a first cause does not automatically prove that logical hierarchies are finite.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi critiques Ibn Sina's doctrine that the final differentia is the first cause and the highest genus is the last effect. In this lesson, you will analyse why this metaphysical claim cannot be used to demonstrate that the series of genera is finite. You will see the distinction between causal chains and conceptual hierarchies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) claims that the final differentia is the first cause of the species, and the highest genus is the last effect in the chain of genera. This is a metaphysical extension of logical concepts.
The claim conflates logical taxonomy with causal hierarchy.
2
Understand the intended use of the claim
An opponent likely uses this claim to argue that the series of ascending genera must be finite, because they end in a first cause (the final differentia) and a last effect (the highest genus).
The argument attempts to prove finitude of genera via causal reasoning.
3
Identify the author's grounds for rejection
Al-Razi states that the demonstration (burhan) only proves that possible things (mumkinat) end in a first cause, not that the chain of genera ends in a last effect. The two are distinct domains.
The causal principle applies to contingent beings, not to conceptual categories.
4
Recognise why the argument fails
The analogy between causal series and generic hierarchy is invalid. Even if there is a first cause, genera could still be infinite in principle because they are not caused in the same way.
The critique highlights the danger of mixing metaphysical and logical orders.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن الفصل فهذا الاحتمال لابد من إبطاله ليتم قولكم. مذهب الشيخ في الفصول والأجناس يقتضي أن كون الفصل الأخير هو العلة الأولى والجنس العالي هو المعلول الأخير، ولا تمكن الاستدلال بذلك على تناهي الأجناس المتصاعدة، لأن البرهان إنما قام على انتهاء الممكنات إلى علة أولى لا إلى معلول آخر.
Concerning the differentia, this probability must be invalidated to complete your statement. The doctrine of the Shaykh regarding the differentiae and genera requires that the final differentia is the first cause and the highest genus is the last effect, and it is not possible to use that as evidence for the finitude of ascending genera, because the demonstration was only established on the ending of possible things to a first cause, not to a last effect.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a critical objection against the view attributed to Ibn Sina (the Shaykh). The author argues that the claim that the final differentia is the first cause and the highest genus is the last effect cannot be used to prove that the series of genera is finite. The philosophical demonstration (burhan) only proves that contingent beings end in a first cause, not that the chain of genera ends in a last effect. Therefore, the analogy is invalid. The author is likely responding to an opponent who used this argument to argue for the finitude of the hierarchy of genera.

Key Takeaway

Introducing the Second Section: Hadd and Rasm
Author transitions to the second section on definition and description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hadd (definition) and rasm (description) and explain why they are essential after mastering the five universals.

Core Idea
Definitions are the keys that open the doors of study: hadd gives essence, rasm gives description.

After completing the five universals, Razi now turns to how these universals are used to define things. The second section is about hadd and rasm—two methods of identifying what something is. This brief transitional passage tells us that definitions are not just academic; they are 'irshadiyya'—guiding matters that open the gates of knowledge and sharpen the mind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the section heading
Find the phrase 'القسم الثاني' in the Arabic. It marks the start of a new major topic. Notice it follows the section on the five universals—this is not random; definitions apply the universals.
The author structures his work so that theory precedes application.
2
Recognize the two key terms: hadd and rasm
The passage explicitly mentions 'الحد' and 'الرسم'. Hadd is a definition that gives the essence (dhat) by genus and differentia. Rasm is a description that uses accidents or properties to identify the thing.
Rasm is often longer but does not penetrate to the essence.
3
Understand the purpose: 'irshadiyya'
The author calls these matters 'irshadiyya'—guiding. Definitions guide beginners to the doors of study. They are not just theoretical; they train the intellect and discipline the soul.
The phrase 'بيطال العقول والنفوس' is obscure; it likely celebrates the lifting of intellects and souls.
4
Connect to the five universals
The five universals (jins, naw', fasl, khassa, 'arad) are the raw material. Hadd uses genus and differentia; rasm uses property and accident. Without the universals, definitions are impossible.
This shows why the earlier section was necessary.

القسم الثاني المقاصر و هو الكلام في الحد الرسم وذلك نعم امور لارشادية الى بواب مفتق الدراسة بيطال العقول والنفوس
The second section, the summaries, and it is discourse on definition and description. That is indeed matters indicative of the gates of the opening of study, the examination of intellects and souls.

Commentary
This brief passage marks the beginning of the second section of the work. The author, after completing the discussion of the five universals (kulliyat khams), now turns to the topic of definition (hadd) and description (rasm). The introductory sentence states that this section deals with 'al-hadd wa al-rasm' (definition and description). The phrase 'al-qism al-thani al-maqasir' likely refers to the second section of concise points. The text describes these as 'irshadiyya' (indicative or guiding) matters that lead to the gates of opening study and examining intellects and souls. This is a standard transitional phrase in classical works, emphasizing the practical utility of logical definitions for attaining knowledge.

Classification of Definitions: Hadd and Rasm
Author divides definitions into essential and accidental, and into complete and incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition as hadd (essential) or rasm (accidental), and complete or incomplete, based on the conditions given by Razi.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be coextensive and better known than the definiendum.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a detailed classification of definitions. He distinguishes between definitions that capture the essence (hadd) and those that describe by accidents (rasm), and further subdivides each into complete and incomplete. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic division
The passage first divides definitions into three types: those using something internal to the quiddity, external, or a combination. Locate these three branches in the Arabic.
Internal refers to essential parts like genus and differentia; external refers to accidents or properties.
2
Analyze the internal branch (hadd)
If the definition uses a part internal to the quiddity, check if that part is concomitant (coextensive) in existence and non-existence. If yes, it is a hadd naqis (incomplete definition). If not, it is unsuitable.
A hadd naqis is incomplete because it does not give the full essence, but it still valid if it names a defining part.
3
Analyze the external branch (rasm)
If the definition is external, check if it is equal in extension and more known. If both conditions hold, it is rasm tam (complete description). If not equal, it is rasm naqis and unsuitable.
Complete description uses a unique accident that belongs only to the definiendum.
4
Handle the combined branch
If the definition uses multiple things with generality and specificity, distinguish the general essential from the specific accidental. The general essential with the specific accidental yields a rasm tam.
The author notes this type has no special name.
5
Avoid circularity
Throughout, ensure the definiendum is not used in the definiens. The passage stresses that a thing cannot be defined by itself, nor by parts that are not better known.
This is a key principle: the definiens must be epistemically prior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني: المقاصد (وهو الكلام في الحد والرسم). تقسيم التعريفات: تعريف الماهية إما أن يكون نفسها وهو محال لان المعرف معلوم قبل المعرف فيلزم أن يكون الشيء معلوما قبل نفسه. وإما بما يكون داخلا فيها أو خارجا عنها أو مركبا منها. فان كان الأول فذلك الجزء: اما ان يكون ملازما لها وجودا وعدما فيكون حدا ناقصا. وإما ان لا يكون كذلك فلا يصلح للتعريف. وان كان الثاني فذلك الخارجي: ان كان مساويا وجودا وعدما وكان أعرف من الماهية كان رسما تاما. وان لم يكن مساويا كان رسما ناقصا ولا يصلح للتعريف. وان كان الثالث فاما ان يكون بين تلك الامور عموم وخصوص اولا. فان كان فالاول: اما ان يكون العام ذاتيا والخاص عرضيا أو بالعكس. الاول هو الرسم التام. وليس له اسم مخصوص. واما تعريف الماهية بجميع اجزائها: اما ان يكون هو نفس الماهية او داخلا فيها او خارجا عنها. الأول محال. الثاني: اما ان يفيد تعريف الماهية بواسطة تعريف الاجزاء او لا. والثاني محال. والاول: اما ان يكون بعد معرفة جميع الاجزاء فيكون تعريفا لشيء بنفسه او قبل معرفتها فيكون تعريفا خارجيا. واما تعريفها بالامور الخارجية فلا يخلو اما ان يكون المطلوب هو نفس الماهية او جزءا او عرضا. والله الموفق.
The second section: The aims (and it is the discussion of definition and description). Division of definitions: The definition of a quiddity is either its very self, which is impossible because the definiendum is known before the definiens, so it would necessitate that a thing is known before itself. Or it is by what is internal to it, or external to it, or composed of them. If the first, then that part: either it is concomitant to it in existence and non-existence, so it is an incomplete definition (hadd naqis). Or it is not like that, so it is not suitable for definition. If the second, then that external thing: if it is equal in existence and non-existence and more known than the quiddity, it is a complete description (rasm tam). If it is not equal, it is an incomplete description and is not suitable for definition. If the third, then either there is generality and specificity among those things or not. If there is, then either the general is essential and the specific is accidental, or vice versa. The first is the complete description. It has no specific name. As for defining the quiddity by all its parts: either it is the quiddity itself, or internal to it, or external to it. The first is impossible. The second: either it gives definition of the quiddity through the definition of the parts or not. The second is impossible. The first: either after knowing all the parts, so it is a definition of a thing by itself, or before knowing them, so it is an external definition. As for defining it by external things, it is either that the sought is the quiddity itself, or a part, or an accident. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a classification of definitions (al-ta'rifat) into types, specifically distinguishing between essential definitions (hadd) and descriptive definitions (rasm). The author begins by stating that a definition of a quiddity (mahiyya) cannot be the quiddity itself, because that would be circular: the definiendum would already have to be known to be defined. Then he divides definitions into those that use something internal to the quiddity, something external, or a combination. If it is internal, it must be a part that is coextensive with the quiddity (concomitant) to be a valid incomplete definition (hadd naqis). Otherwise it is useless. If external, the defining property must be equal in extension and better known to be a complete description (rasm tam). If not equal, it is incomplete description (rasm naqis) and unsuitable. If the definition uses multiple things with a general-specific relation, the general essential with the specific accidental gives a complete description. The author then discusses defining a quiddity by all its parts, which reduces to either circularity or external definition. Finally, he mentions that definition by external things either seeks the quiddity itself, a part, or an accident. Throughout, the author emphasizes that a valid definition must avoid circularity and must use something better known or coextensive.

Key Terms

الرسم
الماهية
تعريف

Checkpoint

A) It must be more general than the quiddity.
B) It must be equal in existence and non-existence and more known than the quiddity.
C) It must be an essential part of the quiddity.
D) It must be a unique property that is not coextensive.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be coextensive with the definiendum and better known; otherwise it is either incomplete or invalid.

Types of Definition: Ḥadd, Rasm, and Their Completeness
Author classifies definitions and argues against self-definition and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as ḥadd tāmm, ḥadd nāqiṣ, rasm tāmm, or rasm nāqiṣ based on whether it uses essential or accidental parts and whether it is coextensive.

Core Idea
Not every coextensive description is a valid definition; only essential definitions reveal the essence.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhaṣ classifies definitions of an essence (māhiyya) into several categories depending on whether the definiens is the essence itself, a part of it, external to it, or a composition. Understanding this classification is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences, such as when jurists define legal terms or theologians define divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the essence (māhiyya) that is being defined in the passage. The definiendum is the thing whose definition is being evaluated.
2
Classify the definiens as internal, external, or composite
Look at the definition: does it use a part of the essence (internal), an accident (external), or a combination? This determines the category of definition.
3
Check coextensiveness and essentiality
If the definiens is internal and coextensive with the essence, it is an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ). If external and coextensive and more known, it is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ). If composite with the general essential and the specific accidental, it is a complete description (rasm tāmm). If none of these, the definition is invalid.
4
Apply the classification to Islamic examples
Take a definition from Fiqh or Kalam and determine its type. For example, defining 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage from grapes' — is it ḥadd or rasm? Is it complete or incomplete?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما تقسيم التعريفات: تعريف الماهية إما أن يكون نفسها وهو محال لأن المعرف معلوم قبل المعرف، فلا يكون المعلوم معلوما قبل نفسه. وإما بما يكون داخلا فيها أو خارجا عنها أو مركبا منها. فإن كان الأول، فذلك الجزء إما أن يكون مساويا لها وجودا وعدما فيكون حدا ناقصا، وإما لا يكون كذلك فلا يصلح للتعريف. وإن كان الثاني، فذلك الخارجي إن كان مساويا وجودا وعدما وكان أعرف من الماهية كان رسما ناقصا ولا يصلح للتعريف. وإن كان الثالث، فإما أن يكون بين تلك الأمور عموم وخصوص، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون العام ذاتيا والخاص عرضيا أو بالعكس؛ الأول هو الرسم التام. وأما تعريف الماهية بلوازمها فإن جميع لوازم الماهية إما أن يكون هو نفس الماهية أو داخلا فيها أو خارجا عنها. والأول محال لأنه تعريف الشيء بنفسه. والثاني محال لأن لوازم الماهية إما أن تكون ذاتية أو عرضية. ولو كان تعريف الماهية ببعض أجزائها فإن ذلك الجزء إما أن يفيد تعريف الماهية بواسطة تعريف جنسها أولا بواسطته. والثاني محال لأن الماهية ليست وراء مجموع أجزائها. والأول يؤدي إلى الدور. وأما تعريفها بالأمور الخارجية فلا يخلو إما أن يكون المطلوب تعريف خصوصية الماهية التي عرض لها ذلك الوصف الخارجي أو تعريف هذا القدر وهو أنه أمر كذا له ذلك الوصف الخارجي. والأول باطل لأن الحقائق المختلفة قد تشترك في لازم واحد فلا يمكن التوصل من ذلك الوصف إلى خصوصية الموصوف إلا إذا قرن بالدليل اختصاص ذلك الوصف بذلك الموصوف، ولكن ذلك لا يمكن معرفته إلا بعد معرفة الموصوف، فلو استفدنا معرفة الموصوف من ذلك الاختصاص لزم الدور وهو محال. والثاني باطل لأنه لو جعلناه تعريفا للخصوصية كان المعروف نفس المعرف وهو محال. واعلم أن هذا السؤال أورده القدماء: أن نعرف المجهول محال؟ أجابوا بأنه لا يمكن طلب الماهيات المجهولة بالكلية، ولكن قد يكون معلوما من وجه ومجهولا من وجه، فلكونه معلوما أمكن توجيه الطلب إليه، ولكونه مجهولا أمكن كونه طالبا لتحصيله.
As for the division of definitions: the definition of an essence is either [1] the essence itself, which is impossible because the defined is known before the definition, so what is known cannot be known before itself. Or [2] by what is internal to it, or external to it, or composed of it. If the first, then that part either equals it in existence and non-existence, so it is an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ), or it is not so, so it is not suitable for definition. If the second, then that external thing, if it equals it in existence and non-existence and is more known than the essence, it is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) and is not suitable for definition. If the third, then there is either generality and specificity between those things; if the first, then either the general is essential and the specific accidental, or vice versa; the first is the complete description (rasm tāmm). As for defining the essence by its necessary concomitants, all the necessary concomitants of the essence are either the essence itself, or internal to it, or external to it. The first is impossible because it is defining a thing by itself. The second is impossible because the necessary concomitants of the essence are either essential or accidental. If the essence is defined by some of its parts, then that part either provides knowledge of the essence via defining its genus or without it. The second is impossible because the essence is nothing beyond the sum of its parts. The first leads to circularity (dawr). As for defining it by external things, it is either that what is sought is the definition of the particularity of the essence that has that external attribute, or the definition of this much: that it is a thing having that external attribute. The first is false because different realities may share a common necessary concomitant, so one cannot arrive from that attribute to the particularity of the described unless it is accompanied by the proof that the attribute is specific to that described, but that cannot be known except after knowing the described; thus if we derived knowledge of the described from that specificity, circularity would follow, which is impossible. The second is false because if we made it a definition of the particularity, then the known would be the same as the definer, which is impossible. And know that this question was raised by the ancients: that it is impossible to know the unknown? They answered that it is not possible to seek essences that are completely unknown, but [an essence] may be known in one respect and unknown in another; because it is known, directing the request towards it is possible, and because it is unknown, one can be a seeker of its acquisition.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of definitions (taʿrīfāt) of an essence (māhiyya). The author first divides definitions into three types: (1) the essence itself—rejected as impossible because it would mean the known is known before itself; (2) by what is internal to the essence (i.e., its parts) or external to it; (3) by what is composed of internal and external. He then examines each type in detail. For internal parts, the part must equal the essence in existence and non-existence to form an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ), otherwise it fails. For external things, they must be equal and more known to form an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ). For compositions with generality and specificity, if the general is essential and the specific accidental, it yields a complete description (rasm tāmm). The author then turns to definitions by necessary concomitants (lawāzim), showing that they cannot be the essence itself or internal without circularity. He explains that defining by a part that requires the genus leads to circularity. He then discusses definitions by external attributes: one cannot define the particular essence by a shared attribute unless that attribute is proved specific, but proving specificity requires prior knowledge of the essence, creating circularity. Finally, he addresses the ancient question of seeking the unknown (ṭalab al-majhūl). The answer is that a thing can be known in one respect (e.g., its genus) and unknown in another (e.g., its differentia), so seeking is possible. This analysis is crucial for understanding valid and invalid definitions, guarding against circularity and incompleteness.

Key Terms
تعريف
ماهية
حد ناقص
رسم ناقص
رسم تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for an internal part to form a valid incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ)?
A) It must be coextensive with the essence.
B) It must be more known than the essence.
C) It must be accidental.
D) It must be composed of general and specific.

Key Takeaway
Definitions are classified based on whether the definiens is essential or accidental and whether it is coextensive with the essence.

Impossibility of Self-Definition and Circularity (Dawr)
Author classifies definitions and argues against self-definition and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circularity (dawr) in definitions and explain why defining a thing by itself or by its necessary concomitants fails.

Core Idea
A definition that presupposes what it defines is no definition at all.

The author argues that defining an essence by itself, by its necessary concomitants, or by external attributes leads to circularity. This analysis is essential for constructing sound definitions and avoiding logical fallacies in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim that self-definition is impossible
The passage states that defining an essence by itself is impossible because the defined must be known prior to the definition, which is contradictory.
2
Analyze definition by necessary concomitants
If the concomitant is the essence itself, it's self-definition; if internal, it's circular; if external, it requires proof of uniqueness which needs prior knowledge of the essence.
3
Examine the argument for circularity via external attributes
To define a particular essence by an external attribute, you must prove that attribute is unique, but that proof requires knowledge of the essence, creating a circle.
4
Understand the solution to the ancient question
The ancients asked how we can seek the unknown. The answer: a thing can be known in one respect (e.g., its genus) and unknown in another (e.g., its differentia), so seeking is possible without circularity.

As for the division of definitions: the definition of an essence is either [1] the essence itself, which is impossible because the defined is known before the definition, so what is known cannot be known before itself. Or [2] by what is internal to it, or external to it, or composed of it. If the first, then that part either equals it in existence and non-existence, so it is an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ), or it is not so, so it is not suitable for definition. If the second, then that external thing, if it equals it in existence and non-existence and is more known than the essence, it is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) and is not suitable for definition. If the third, then there is either generality and specificity between those things; if the first, then either the general is essential and the specific accidental, or vice versa; the first is the complete description (rasm tāmm). As for defining the essence by its necessary concomitants, all the necessary concomitants of the essence are either the essence itself, or internal to it, or external to it. The first is impossible because it is defining a thing by itself. The second is impossible because the necessary concomitants of the essence are either essential or accidental. If the essence is defined by some of its parts, then that part either provides knowledge of the essence via defining its genus or without it. The second is impossible because the essence is nothing beyond the sum of its parts. The first leads to circularity (dawr). As for defining it by external things, it is either that what is sought is the definition of the particularity of the essence that has that external attribute, or the definition of this much: that it is a thing having that external attribute. The first is false because different realities may share a common necessary concomitant, so one cannot arrive from that attribute to the particularity of the described unless it is accompanied by the proof that the attribute is specific to that described, but that cannot be known except after knowing the described; thus if we derived knowledge of the described from that specificity, circularity would follow, which is impossible. The second is false because if we made it a definition of the particularity, then the known would be the same as the definer, which is impossible. And know that this question was raised by the ancients: that it is impossible to know the unknown? They answered that it is not possible to seek essences that are completely unknown, but [an essence] may be known in one respect and unknown in another; because it is known, directing the request towards it is possible, and because it is unknown, one can be a seeker of its acquisition.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of definitions (taʿrīfāt) of an essence (māhiyya). The author first divides definitions into three types: (1) the essence itself—rejected as impossible because it would mean the known is known before itself; (2) by what is internal to the essence (i.e., its parts) or external to it; (3) by what is composed of internal and external. He then examines each type in detail. For internal parts, the part must equal the essence in existence and non-existence to form an incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ), otherwise it fails. For external things, they must be equal and more known to form an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ). For compositions with generality and specificity, if the general is essential and the specific accidental, it yields a complete description (rasm tāmm). The author then turns to definitions by necessary concomitants (lawāzim), showing that they cannot be the essence itself or internal without circularity. He explains that defining by a part that requires the genus leads to circularity. He then discusses definitions by external attributes: one cannot define the particular essence by a shared attribute unless that attribute is proved specific, but proving specificity requires prior knowledge of the essence, creating circularity. Finally, he addresses the ancient question of seeking the unknown (ṭalab al-majhūl). The answer is that a thing can be known in one respect (e.g., its genus) and unknown in another (e.g., its differentia), so seeking is possible. This analysis is crucial for understanding valid and invalid definitions, guarding against circularity and incompleteness.

Key Terms
ماهية
لازم
دور
تعريف الشيء بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does defining an essence by its necessary concomitant lead to circularity if the concomitant is external?
A) The concomitant is always the essence itself.
B) Proving the concomitant's uniqueness requires prior knowledge of the essence.
C) The concomitant is always accidental.
D) The concomitant is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Defining a thing by itself or by something that depends on it creates circularity, making the definition invalid.
