Refuting Definitions by External Attributes
Author refutes two incorrect ways of defining a quiddity using an external attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute two invalid methods of defining a quiddity: (1) using an external attribute as a definer, and (2) making the definer identical to the definiendum.

Core Idea
A quiddity cannot be defined by an external attribute or by itself.

Al-Razi critiques two flawed ways to define a quiddity (mahiyya) when it has an external attribute. The first defines it as 'the specific nature that has that attribute', the second as 'the thing that has that attribute'. Both fail logically: the first leads to circularity (dawr), the second to self-reference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two false definitions
The passage presents two attempted definitions: (1) defining the specific quiddity that possesses an external attribute, and (2) defining it simply as 'the thing that has that attribute'.
Both definitions rely on the external attribute to identify the quiddity.
2
Analyze the first refutation
Al-Razi argues that different quiddities can share the same necessary attribute (lazim). Therefore, you cannot use that attribute alone to pinpoint the specific quiddity. Even if the attribute is proven unique, proving that uniqueness requires prior knowledge of the quiddity, causing circularity.
The logical error is that the attribute (lazim) is not exclusive to one quiddity, and uniqueness would create a circular dependency.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If knowing the attribute's uniqueness requires knowing the quiddity, and we claim to know the quiddity through that uniqueness, we have a circle (dawr). This is logically impossible.
Circular definitions are invalid in logic.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Defining a quiddity as 'the thing that has attribute X' makes the definer (the quiddity) identical to the definiendum (itself). But a valid definition requires the definer to be prior and distinct from the definiendum.
This is a form of self-reference (defining a thing by itself).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف خصوصية الماهية التي عرض لها ذلك الوصف الخارجي، أو تعريف هذا القدر وهو أنه أمر كذا له ذلك الوصف الخارجي. والأول باطل، لأن الحقائق المختلفة قد تشترك في لازم واحد، فلا يمكن التوصل من ذلك الوصف إلى خصوصية الموصوف، اللهم إلا أن يكون قد ثبت بالدليل اختصاص ذلك الوصف بذلك الموصوف، ولكن ذلك ما لا يمكن معرفته إلا بعد معرفة الموصوف، فلو استفدنا معرفة الموصوف من ذلك الاختصاص لزم الدور وهو محال. والثاني أيضا باطل، لأن الشيء لو جعلناه معرفا لنفسه، فالشرط كونه معلومًا قبل المعرف، والخصوصية ذلك الشيء، كان المعروف نفس المعرف وهو محال.
Defining the specific quiddity that has that external attribute, or defining this amount (i.e., that it is a thing that has that external attribute). The first is false, because different realities may share a single necessary attribute, so it is not possible to arrive from that attribute to the specific nature of the described, unless it has been established by evidence that the attribute is specific to that described. But that cannot be known except after knowing the described; so if we derive knowledge of the described from that specificity, circularity results, which is impossible. The second is also false, because if we make the thing a definer of itself, then the condition is that it be known before the definer, and the specific aspect of that thing would be the known itself, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage critiques two false approaches to defining a quiddity (mahiyya) that is associated with an external attribute (wasf khariji). The first approach defines it as the specific nature that has that attribute; the second defines it simply as 'the thing that has that attribute.' The author rejects both: the first fails because different quiddities can share the same attribute, and even if uniqueness were known, it would require prior knowledge of the quiddity, causing circularity. The second fails because it would make the definer identical to the definiendum, violating the principle that the definer must be prior and distinct. This is a typical logical critique of improper definitions.

B) Because it leads to circularity or self-reference.
C) Because external attributes are unknown.
D) Because quiddities cannot be defined at all.

Key Takeaway
Defining a quiddity by a non-essential attribute (lazim) leads to circularity or self-reference, making the definition invalid.

The Ancient Doubt and Definition by Example
Author answers the philosophers' objection that defining the unknown is impossible, and explains definition by example as a descriptive definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq in the context of definition, and identify definition by example (ta'rif bi al-mithal) as a type of rasm.

Core Idea
Defining the unknown is possible through descriptive definitions that rely on necessary concomitants.

The ancient philosophers argued that defining the unknown is contradictory. In this passage, Razi acknowledges the logical difficulty but shows how definition by example circumvents it, by using a necessary concomitant (lazim) that is already known.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ancient doubt
Find the objection: defining an unknown conception (tasawwur) seems impossible because you cannot seek what you don't know.
2
Analyze the answer for tasdiqat
The ancients answered for assertoric demands: the conceptions are already known; the demand is their relation. But this fails for tasawwurat.
Tasdiqat: judgments; tasawwurat: concepts.
3
Extract Razi's own answer
Definition by example is a rasm because it uses a resemblance (necessary concomitant) that is known, not the essence itself.
Resemblance is a lazim of the quiddity's specific nature.
4
Apply to a real example
Use an Islamic example: defining "wine" (khamr) by giving the example of "nabidh" because both share the 'illah of intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا السؤال أورده القدماء، أن تعريف المجهول محال، وأجابوا: بأن المطالب التصديقية، بأنه إذا طلبنا أن العالم هل هو محدث أم لا، نتصور العالم والحدوث حاصلان، والطلب هو نسبة أحدهما بالإيجاب أو السلب إلى الآخر، فإذا وجد المطلوب علمنا أن الذي وجدنا هو الذي طلبناه بواسطة التصورات التي كانت معلومة قبل ذلك. لكن هذا الجواب لا يلائم التصورات، فإن التصور الذي تطلبه إن لم يكن حاصلا عندك استحال أن تطلب تحصيله، لأن الطالب إن كان عاقلا استحال أن يطلبه سواء حصل عنده تصور سواه أو لم يحصل، وإن كان حاصلا استحال طلبه أيضا. وأما أن نجيب عن الأقسام: التعريف بالمثال تعريف رسمي، لأن المثال شابه للممثل من وجه فلك المشابهة لازم من لوازم خاصية الماهية، فتعريفها بها تعريف بوصف خارجي. وعن الثاني: أنا لا نعني بالمسمل إلا تفصيل ما دل عليه الاسم إجمالا. وهو الجواب عن الثالث. وعلى هذا الوجه تقع الشكوكات في تقسيم الماهيات بحسب الحد الفاعل أربعة أقسام: فالمُركب الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره، فإنه يُحَدُّ لتركبه ولا يُحَدُّ به لكونه ليس من أمهات الماهيات. والمُركب الذي يتركب عنه غيره، يُحَدُّ لتركبه ويُحَدُّ به لتركب غيره عنه. والبسيط الذي يتركب عنه غيره، لا يُحَدُّ لبساطته ويُحَدُّ به لتركب غيره عنه. والبسيط الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره، لا يُحَدُّ لبساطته ولا يُحَدُّ به لعدم التركيب. وقد ظهر من التقرير أن البسيط إما أن لا يكون متصورا أصلا، أو إن كان كان غنيا عن الاكتساب في الأمور المتصورة. قال: لا غني عن الاكتساب كل تصور يفتقر عليه تصديق أولى كان بالأولوية أولى. ومن المعلوم أن القضايا المجرية والوجدانية العالية كالألوان والأضواء والأصوات والطعوم والدفء والملموسات، وكذا العوارض: القدرة والإرادة والشهوة والنغم والألم واللذة والسرور والغضب وأشباهها.
Know that this doubt was raised by the ancients, that defining the unknown is impossible. They answered regarding assertoric demands (tasdiqat) that when we seek whether the world is originated or not, we conceive the world and origination, and the demand is the relation of one to the other by affirmation or negation. So when we find the sought, we know that what we found is what we sought by means of the conceptions that were known before. But this answer does not suit conceptions (tasawwurat), because the conception you seek, if it is not already in your possession, it is impossible to seek its acquisition, because the seeker, if he is rational, cannot seek it whether he has another conception or not; and if it is already in possession, seeking it is also impossible. As for our answer about the sections: definition by example (ta'rif bi al-mithal) is a rasm (descriptive definition), because the example resembles the exemplified in some respect, so that resemblance is one of the necessary concomitants of the specific nature of the quiddity, so defining it by that is definition by an external attribute. Regarding the second: we mean by 'musmal' only the detailing of what the name indicates in summary. This is the answer to the third. And on this basis, the doubts occur in the division of quiddities according to the efficient cause into four kinds: (1) the composite that is not composed from something else: it is defined because of its composition, but it is not used to define because it is not among the primary quiddities. (2) The composite from which something else is composed: it is defined because of its composition and used to define because others are composed from it. (3) The simple from which something else is composed: it is not defined because of its simplicity, but it is used to define because others are composed from it. (4) The simple from which nothing else is composed: it is not defined because of its simplicity, nor is it used to define because there is no composition. It has become clear from the discussion that the simple is either not conceived at all, or if it is conceived, it is independent of acquisition in the things conceived. He said: No conception that requires an assent for its correction is independent of acquisition; the prior is more prior. It is known that the primary and immediate propositions such as colors, lights, sounds, tastes, warmth, tangible objects, and also attributes like power, will, appetite, melody, pain, pleasure, joy, anger and the like.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage addresses an ancient skeptical objection: defining the unknown is impossible. The ancient philosophers responded by focusing on tasdiqat (assertoric demands), where the demand involves a relation between already known conceptions. However, this does not apply to tasawwurat (conceptual demands), because if the conception is not already present, seeking it is impossible. The author provides his own answer: definition by example is a rasm (descriptive definition), because the resemblance between example and exemplified is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the quiddity's specific nature, so it is a definition by an external attribute. This resolves the doubts. The passage then presents a famous classification of quiddities (mahiyat) into four categories based on composition and definability: (1) composite that does not compose others: it can be defined but not used to define; (2) composite that composes others: can be defined and used to define; (3) simple that composes others: cannot be defined but can be used to define; (4) simple that does not compose others: neither defined nor used to define. Finally, it notes that simple quiddities are either inconceivable or conceived independently, and that any conception requiring a prior tasdiq is dependent. Examples of primary propositions are sensory and experiential data (colors, sounds, etc.). This is a key passage for understanding the limits and types of definition.

Key Terms
تعريف بالمثال
رسم
حد
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main limitation of the philosophers' answer to the ancient doubt?
A) It only works for tasdiqat, not for tasawwurat.
B) It only works for tasawwurat, not for tasdiqat.
C) It is invalid for both types.
D) It is valid for both types.

Key Takeaway
Definition by example is a valid rasm that relies on a known necessary concomitant, resolving the paradox of defining the unknown.

Classification of Quiddities into Four Types
Author divides quiddities based on composition and definability into four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any quiddity into one of the four categories based on composition and definability.

Core Idea
Quiddities are either simple or composite, and their definability depends on whether they compose other quiddities.

After addressing the possibility of definition, Razi presents a systematic classification of quiddities based on two axes: composition (simple/composite) and whether they are used to define other quiddities. This classification is essential for understanding the hierarchy of concepts in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The classification uses two criteria: whether the quiddity is simple (basit) or composite (murakkab), and whether it composes other quiddities (i.e., is a part of other quiddities).
Simple: no parts; Composite: has parts.
2
List the four types with examples
Type 1: Composite that does not compose others (e.g., 'human' – defined but not used to define). Type 2: Composite that composes others (e.g., 'animal' – defined and used to define). Type 3: Simple that composes others (e.g., 'existence' – used to define but not defined). Type 4: Simple that does not compose others (e.g., 'color' – neither defined nor used to define).
Examples from Islamic sciences: 'knowledge' (simple, composes others).
3
Determine the category of a given term
Take a term from Islamic science, e.g., 'qudra' (power). Is it simple or composite? Does it compose others? Apply the criteria.
4
Explain why simple quiddities cannot be defined
Simple quiddities have no genus or differentia, so they cannot be given a hadd (essential definition). They can only be pointed to or described.

Rasm: Definition by External Description
Definition of rasm and defense of definition by example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a definition by external description (rasm) from a real definition (hadd) and identify when an example-based definition is a rasm.

Core Idea
Defining by example is a rasm because similarity is an external property.

Al-Razi begins by addressing an objection: how can definition by example be a valid definition? He argues that it is a type of rasm, not hadd, because the similarity between the example and the defined thing is an external attribute, not part of the essence. This lesson unpacks how rasm works and why it is less complete than hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the example
Find the term being defined and the example used to define it. In the passage, Al-Razi implies that defining by example uses an external likeness (shabah).
For instance, defining 'wine' by showing a glass of juice is not a definition of essence.
2
Determine if the example shares an essential or accidental property
The similarity between the example and the defined is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the example's quiddity, not an essential part of the defined. Therefore, it is an external description.
3
Classify the definition as rasm
Since the definition uses a property that is not part of the essence, it is a rasm (description), not a hadd (definition proper). According to Al-Razi, this is a valid form of definition but incomplete.
Rasm can be complete or incomplete; this one is a rasm naqis.
4
Compare with hadd using Al-Razi's criteria
Hadd gives a conceptual form that matches the reality itself; rasm does not. Rasm is subject to increase or decrease, while hadd is fixed.
Al-Razi later states that hadd is more complete than rasm in two respects.

We answer the objections: definition by example is a rasm definition because the example resembles the exemplified in some respect, and similarity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of the example, so defining by it is a definition by an external description. Second: we mean by the equivalent (musalsal) only a detailed statement of what the name implies in summary, and that is the answer to the third. On this basis, the knots are cut in the division of quiddities according to the agent definition (hadhfa'il) into four kinds: the composite which is not composed from it of another (that is its definition), because it is only known after knowing the genus and difference, and it is not a definition by it that it is not from a quiddity other than... the simple that has no definition due to its simplicity, and it is defined by it because it is from the kinds of... the simples that are not defined by anything due to their simplicity, and they are not defined by others because they have no parts from others. And it has appeared from the expositions that the definition (hadd) is six: either it is not originally known, or if it is known, it is independent of acquisition in the known matters, leading to being independent of acquisition; every concept that a first assent is built upon is more prior in priority. And it is known that the absolute and immediate principles (qadaya mujarrada and wijdaniyya) — colors, lights, sounds, tastes, touches, and likewise faculties like power, will, desire, enjoyment, pain, etc. — are things that cannot be defined except by changing a word for a clearer word, due to their obviousness and because they are of clear existence, more known than immediate perceptions and sensibles. Then it is said: it may be that not everyone who knows a thing by mentioning its parts has come to know it by definition; if it is known by descriptions (rusum), the quiddity known thereby is depicted (marsuma), not defined (mahduda), because the quiddity is nothing but the sum of those parts. And if each one of them is not conceived in itself, but only a necessary concomitant of it, then the quiddity in itself is not conceived; rather, what is conceived is a collection of things each of which is a concomitant of one of the parts of the quiddity. So the concept obtained is a composite property. That definition (hadd) is not acquired by argument (hujja), because the definition is a detailed statement of what the name implies summarily, and that is not subject to dispute except linguistically, and it is not rational, and it is also dispute in assent (tasdiq). And because the definition is the sum of its essential attributes, and it is required that it [the definition] be more known than the sum of the essential attributes of the defined thing, and it must be so, therefore the definition is not derived from argument. This is if the definition is a definitive statement (hadd mahd) that defines the reality; as for if it is not a definitive statement, it points to a particular existent and claims its reality is composed of such-and-such, then it must have proof. The increase on the definition is not possible, and on the description is possible. As for the first: because it is an expression of the sum of the parts of that thing, and that is not subject to increase or decrease, because the increase on the mentioned would be a part of the whole, but the mentioned is the sum of the parts, and it would not be a mention of an increase on the definition. From this it appears that the quiddity has no more than one definition, and that one ignorant of the definition is ignorant of the defined, and one knowing it knows the defined necessarily. As for the second: because it mentions the properties and external attributes of the thing, and that is subject to increase and decrease. In the comparison between definitions and descriptions, the definition is more complete than the description in two respects: first, because it provides a conceptual form (tasawwur) that corresponds to the thing itself, while the description does not; and as for the division of the description, the external description does not produce knowledge of the quiddity unless it is present in the mind. Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition on the nature of definition (hadd) and description (rasm). The author begins by answering objections: definition by example is a rasm definition because similarity is an external attribute. Then he divides quiddities (mahiyyat) into four types based on how they relate to definition: some are composite and define others, some are simple and cannot define others, etc. He then lists six types of definition, though the text is corrupt; it seems to distinguish between what is originally known and what is acquired. He notes that simple sensations and internal states (like colors, sounds, pains) cannot be defined except by synonym. Then he argues that if a quiddity is known through its parts, but the parts themselves are not conceived, the resulting knowledge is merely a collection of concomitants, not a true definition. He asserts that hadd is not derivable from proof (hujja) because it is merely an analysis of what the name means, and that dispute about it is only linguistic. He then contrasts hadd and rasm: hadd is complete and cannot be increased or decreased, while rasm can vary; hadd provides a mental form matching the reality, whereas rasm does not. The passage aims to show the limitations of definitions in producing new knowledge.

لازم

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi consider definition by example a rasm rather than a hadd?
A) Because the example is a particular instance of the defined.
B) Because the similarity between the example and the defined is an external property, not an essential part of the defined.
C) Because definition by example is not a recognized type of definition.
D) Because the example is always a simpler concept.

Key Takeaway
Definition by example is a rasm because it uses an external property (similarity) and thus does not capture the essence of the defined thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition in a fiqh text that uses an example. Explain why it is a rasm and not a hadd, using Al-Razi's criteria.
?
Construct a rasm definition for 'salah' (prayer) using an external description (e.g., actions like bowing and prostrating) and explain its limitations compared to a hadd definition.

Division of Quiddities: Composites and Simples
Division of quiddities into four types according to definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a quiddity into one of four types based on its compositeness and ability to define other things.

Core Idea
Quiddities are divided into four kinds: those that define others, those that are defined by others, those that do both, and those that do neither.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a fourfold division of quiddities (mahiyyat) according to how they relate to definition. Some quiddities are composite and can define or be defined; others are simple and cannot be defined at all. This lesson helps you navigate this classification and understand its implications for definition theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types from the passage
Al-Razi says quiddities are divided into four kinds according to the 'agent definition' (hadh fa'il). The text lists: composite that defines another, simple that is defined by another, simple that neither defines nor is defined, and a fourth type that is composite and defined by another? (text corrupt). Focus on the general scheme.
The corrupt text mentions 'the composite which is not composed from it of another' meaning it defines others, and 'the simple that has no definition'.
2
Classify each type as definable or not
Composite quiddities (like 'human') can be defined by genus and differentia. Simple quiddities (like 'existence') cannot be defined because they have no parts. Some simples may serve as genera for others.
3
Apply the division to a known example
Take 'human' as a composite quiddity: it has parts (animal, rational). It defines individual humans. 'Whiteness' is a simple quality: it cannot be defined because it is a simple accident.
4
Understand why simples cannot be defined
Al-Razi states that simples have no definition due to their simplicity; they are known directly through sensation or immediate awareness (wijdan).
This connects to his later point about colors, sounds, etc., being indefinable except by synonym.

Hadd and Rasm

Al-Razi's Critique: Hadd Is Not Derived from Proof
Critique of the epistemic value of definitions and argument that hadd is not derived from proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate Al-Razi's argument that a real definition (hadd) is not derived from a proof (hujja) and that disputes about definitions are merely linguistic.

Core Idea
A real definition is an analysis of a word's meaning, not a conclusion of a proof.

In this challenging passage, Al-Razi argues that a proper definition (hadd) does not come from a syllogism or proof. Instead, it is a detailed statement of what a name implies summarily. Disagreement about definitions is only about language, not rational demonstration. This lesson unpacks this radical critique and its implications for the epistemological status of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Extract Al-Razi's claim about hadd and hujja
Al-Razi says 'al-hadd ghayr maktasab bi al-hujja' (the definition is not acquired by proof). He argues that hadd is just an explication of the meaning of a name, so any dispute is linguistic, not rational.
2
Identify his supporting reason
He says the definition is the sum of essential attributes (dhatiyyat) of the defined, and these attributes must be better known than the defined. If they were not, you could not define through them. But this condition makes definition prior to proof.
3
Contrast with rasm
Unlike hadd, rasm is based on external descriptions and can vary; it does not provide a true conceptual match. Hadd gives a tasawwur that corresponds to the thing itself.
4
Evaluate the force of the critique
Al-Razi is challenging the idea that definitions can be proven; they are merely analytical. This implies that strict definitions cannot generate new knowledge, only clarify existing concepts.
This is a significant epistemological claim in the Islamic logical tradition.

Source Text

We answer the objections: definition by example is a rasm definition because the example resembles the exemplified in some respect, and similarity is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of the example, so defining by it is a definition by an external description. Second: we mean by the equivalent (musalsal) only a detailed statement of what the name implies in summary, and that is the answer to the third. On this basis, the knots are cut in the division of quiddities according to the agent definition (hadhfa'il) into four kinds: the composite which is not composed from it of another (that is its definition), because it is only known after knowing the genus and difference, and it is not a definition by it that it is not from a quiddity other than... the simple that has no definition due to its simplicity, and it is defined by it because it is from the kinds of... the simples that are not defined by anything due to their simplicity, and they are not defined by others because they have no parts from others. And it has appeared from the expositions that the definition (hadd) is six: either it is not originally known, or if it is known, it is independent of acquisition in the known matters, leading to being independent of acquisition; every concept that a first assent is built upon is more prior in priority. And it is known that the absolute and immediate principles (qadaya mujarrada and wijdaniyya) — colors, lights, sounds, tastes, touches, and likewise faculties like power, will, desire, enjoyment, pain, etc. — are things that cannot be defined except by changing a word for a clearer word, due to their obviousness and because they are of clear existence, more known than immediate perceptions and sensibles. Then it is said: it may be that not everyone who knows a thing by mentioning its parts has come to know it by definition; if it is known by descriptions (rusum), the quiddity known thereby is depicted (marsuma), not defined (mahduda), because the quiddity is nothing but the sum of those parts. And if each one of them is not conceived in itself, but only a necessary concomitant of it, then the quiddity in itself is not conceived; rather, what is conceived is a collection of things each of which is a concomitant of one of the parts of the quiddity. So the concept obtained is a composite property. That definition (hadd) is not acquired by argument (hujja), because the definition is a detailed statement of what the name implies summarily, and that is not subject to dispute except linguistically, and it is not rational, and it is also dispute in assent (tasdiq). And because the definition is the sum of its essential attributes, and it is required that it [the definition] be more known than the sum of the essential attributes of the defined thing, and it must be so, therefore the definition is not derived from argument. This is if the definition is a definitive statement (hadd mahd) that defines the reality; as for if it is not a definitive statement, it points to a particular existent and claims its reality is composed of such-and-such, then it must have proof. The increase on the definition is not possible, and on the description is possible. As for the first: because it is an expression of the sum of the parts of that thing, and that is not subject to increase or decrease, because the increase on the mentioned would be a part of the whole, but the mentioned is the sum of the parts, and it would not be a mention of an increase on the definition. From this it appears that the quiddity has no more than one definition, and that one ignorant of the definition is ignorant of the defined, and one knowing it knows the defined necessarily. As for the second: because it mentions the properties and external attributes of the thing, and that is subject to increase and decrease. In the comparison between definitions and descriptions, the definition is more complete than the description in two respects: first, because it provides a conceptual form (tasawwur) that corresponds to the thing itself, while the description does not; and as for the division of the description, the external description does not produce knowledge of the quiddity unless it is present in the mind. But the high...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition on the nature of definition (hadd) and description (rasm). The author begins by answering objections: definition by example is a rasm definition because similarity is an external attribute. Then he divides quiddities (mahiyyat) into four types based on how they relate to definition: some are composite and define others, some are simple and cannot define others, etc. He then lists six types of definition, though the text is corrupt; it seems to distinguish between what is originally known and what is acquired. He notes that simple sensations and internal states (like colors, sounds, pains) cannot be defined except by synonym. Then he argues that if a quiddity is known through its parts, but the parts themselves are not conceived, the resulting knowledge is merely a collection of concomitants, not a true definition. He asserts that hadd is not derivable from proof (hujja) because it is merely an analysis of what the name means, and that dispute about it is only linguistic. He then contrasts hadd and rasm: hadd is complete and cannot be increased or decreased, while rasm can vary; hadd provides a mental form matching the reality, whereas rasm does not. The passage aims to show the limitations of definitions in producing new knowledge.

Key Terms
حد
حجة
تصديق
لفظي

B) Because a hadd is a detailed statement of what a name implies, so disagreement is about the meaning of the name, not about a reality.
C) Because definitions cannot be changed.
D) Because definitions are based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that real definitions are not conclusions of proofs but clarifications of word meanings, limiting their epistemic role.

Hadd and Rasm

Hadd and Rasm: Essence and Description
Author defines the epistemic status of hadd and rasm in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a definition by essence (hadd) and a description by accident (rasm), and explain why a true hadd cannot be proven by evidence.

Core Idea
A true definition (hadd) is not proven; its parts are more known than the whole.

In this passage, al-Razi makes a crucial epistemological point: a real definition (hadd) does not require proof because its essential parts (jins and fasl) are already known to the mind. In contrast, a description (rasm) only conveys accidental properties without penetrating the essence. Understanding this distinction is fundamental for anyone who wants to construct valid definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
What is al-Razi's central thesis about hadd and hujja? He states that hadd is not known by evidence (hujja) because it is a detailed explanation of a name by its essential parts, not subject to dispute except linguistically.
2
Distinguish hadd from rasm
Contrast the two types of definition: hadd states the essence (mahiyya) through essential parts (jins, fasl), while rasm identifies the thing through accidental properties or external characteristics, describing without penetrating reality.
In rasm, the essence is 'described, not limited' (مرسومة لا محدودة).
3
Analyze the epistemic reasoning
Why does al-Razi say hadd is not known by evidence? Because the essential parts must be more known than the composite whole; if the parts themselves are not directly conceived, the essence remains unknown. Thus hadd is immediate conception, not inferential assent.
This ties to the distinction between tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'wudu' as 'washing specific limbs with water'. Its parts (washing, limbs, water) are individually known before the composite act, making it a potential hadd. If defined by its effects (e.g., purification), it becomes a rasm.
5
Check the logical consequence
If hadd requires prior knowledge of its parts, what does that imply about the starting points of knowledge? They are axiomatic and self-evident, not derived from proof. This is foundational in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امور لا يمكن تعريفها الاعاسيل تبديل لفظ بلفظ أوضح منه نفهما للشامل ولانه لبيسة الوجود شيخ اعرف من الوجدانيات والمحسوسافحة نمر فيا بيا كرة إنه ليس كل من عرف التي بذكر اجزانه فقدت في بالحدلات الاجو آ اذا كانت معرفة بالرسوم كانت الماهية المعرفة بها مرسومة لا محدوة لان لا الماهية ليس الا مجموع تلك. الاجزا و اذا لم تكن كل واحد منها متصورا في نفسه بالمتصور لازم من لوازمه كانت الماهية في نفسها غير متصورة بل المصور منها مجموع اموركات واحد منها لازم لحل واحد من اجل الماهبة فالمفهوم من الحاصل خاصة مركبة من ان الحمد عير مكتب بالحبة لان الحد ليبرال تفصيل مادك لاسم عليه بالاعمال وذلك ما لا مكن وقوع النزاع فيه الآمن جهة اللغة وذلك لبر عن عقليا ولانه ايضا نزاع في التصديق ولان حد الته مجموع ذاتیاته ويتخير ان كون للنے س اعرف من مجموع الحجة ذاتياته له ويجب كونها كذلك فالحد غير مستفاد من الحجة
Things that cannot be defined except by exchanging a word for a clearer word in understanding the comprehensive [meaning], and because it is not of the nature of existence as something more known than immediate experiences and sensed things... It is not the case that everyone who defines a thing by mentioning its parts has made a definition. If the definition is by description (rasm), then the essence defined is described, not limited (by hadd), because the essence is nothing but the sum of those parts. And if each one of them is not itself conceived in itself, but rather a necessary concomitant of its concomitants, then the essence in itself is not conceived; rather what is conceived is a collection of things each of which is a concomitant of one of the parts of the essence. So the concept obtained is a composite property. Therefore, hadd is not known by evidence (hujja), because the definition is a detailed explanation of a name by means of its essential parts, and that is not subject to dispute except linguistically, and that is a mental matter, and it is also a dispute about assent (tasdiq). And because the definition is the sum of its essential parts, and it is necessary that the essential parts are more known than the total of the essence's essential parts, therefore the definition is not derived from evidence.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between definition by essence (hadd) and definition by description (rasm). The author argues that a true hadd cannot be proven by external evidence (hujja) because its component parts (essentialia) must be already known and more clear than the whole. If we define through accidents or external properties (rasm), we only describe the essence without penetrating its reality. The author emphasizes that the epistemic foundation of hadd is immediate conception (tasawwur) of the parts, not inferential assent (tasdiq). The argument follows a logical chain: the essence is known only through its parts; if the parts themselves are not directly conceived, the essence remains unknown. Hence, a proper hadd does not require a proof—it is the starting point for knowledge. An example from Islamic sciences: the definition of 'wudu' as 'washing specific limbs with water'—its parts (washing, limbs, water) are individually known before the composite act.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): A definition that states the essence (mahiyya) of a thing by mentioning its essential parts (jins and fasl). It is not subject to proof because its parts are more known. [Note: In earlier logic (e.g., Isaghuji), hadd includes both complete and incomplete definitions; here al-Razi emphasizes its epistemic immediacy.]
الرسم (al-rasm): A description that identifies a thing through its accidental properties or external characteristics, not its essence. [Note: No significant shift—consistent across Mantiq works.]
الحجة (al-hujja): Evidence or proof that leads to assent (tasdiq), not applicable to hadd because hadd is a tasawwur of essence. [Note: In Usul al-Fiqh, hujja often refers to a legal proof; here it is epistemological.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is a true definition (hadd) not known by evidence (hujja)?
A) Because definitions are arbitrary linguistic conventions.
B) Because the parts of the definition are more known than the defined, so no proof is needed.
C) Because evidence only applies to sensible objects.
D) Because definitions are based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
A true definition (hadd) is an immediate conception of essence, not a conclusion from evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of salah (prayer) as 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Is this a hadd or a rasm? Identify its essential parts if any.
?
Examine the definition of 'wudu' as 'washing the face, hands, head, and feet with water'. Is this a hadd or rasm? What would be needed to make it a hadd?

Why Hadd Cannot Be Increased or Decreased
Al-Razi explains the impossibility of increase and decrease in hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why increase and decrease are impossible in a hadd and identify the necessary parts of a definition in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Hadd is the complete sum of essential parts; any addition or omission renders it invalid.

Al-Razi argues that a true definition must include all and only the essential characteristics of a thing. In this lesson, we examine his reasoning that a hadd cannot be increased or decreased because it already contains the full essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about hadd
Locate in the passage where al-Razi says that increase and decrease are impossible in hadd. He states: 'الزيادة على الحد غير ممكنة' and 'النقصان غير ممكن'.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why would addition be impossible? Because hadd is 'ذكر مجموع الأجزاء' (mention of the sum of parts). Adding anything would mean the original sum was incomplete, contradicting the definition. Omission would likewise miss an essential part.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the hadd of salah: 'specific statements and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. If we add 'raising hands' it is extraneous; if we omit 'takbir' it is deficient. Both cases violate the completeness of hadd.
This example is from later commentary, but illustrates the principle.
4
Distinguish from rasm
Note that rasm, unlike hadd, can be increased or decreased because it lists external attributes which are not fixed.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا اذا كان الحد مكتب الام اما اذا لكان عبر الحقيقة وهوان تشير إلى موجود معين وتزعم ان حقيقة مركبة من كذا وكذا فلا انه لابد فيه من الكحة قذان الزيادة على الحدغر ممكنة وعلى الرسم مكنة انا الأول فلانه عبارة عن ذكر مجموع اجوا الني وذلك غير قابل للزيادة والنقصان لان الزائد على المذكورات كان جوا الم لكن المذكور اقلاً. مجموع الأجزا و الألم يكن ذكر زيادة لحد ومن هذا يظهران الماهية الوان ليتها للا الحد الواحد وان الجاهل الحد جاهل بالمحدود والعالم به عالم به لا محالة واما الثاني فلانة و ذکر خواص الشر وصفاته الخارجية وذلك قابل للزيادة والنقصان في المناسبة بين الحدود والرسوم الحد اتم من الرسم الوجهين امتنا إلى الرسم الاسلام في الرسم از يذكر دفعة واحدة صحيح اجراست لا متاع إن تكون لله رسوم مائة بل يريد به الرسم من حيث مورسم اولا فلاته لعيد تصو ر ل مطابقًا للشريخ نفسه والرسم لانفيه ذلك واما انگار :: القسم السلام فلات القصف الخارجي لا يفيد معرفة النته الا اذا كان حاصلا لها العين لكن العالية الوصف الغالاتي كه موقوف على العلميه فلي استفد فا العلم به من نشوف ذلك الوصف الزم المدور واما العلامات ذلك الوصف عنر حاصل الغيره فلانة الحصر الا بعد لم بكل ما يغايره والأسوار المغايرة غير متناهية فيلزم توقف العلم به على العالما لانهاية له وهو محال وانتا الرسم فانه اسم من الحملات البساط الحدود ها البتة
This is if the definition is limited. But if it is not, then [the definition] refers to a specific existent and claims that its reality is composed of such-and-such. Then it must have evidence. Increase in hadd is impossible, but in rasm it is possible. As for the first: because it is an expression of the sum of the parts, and that is not subject to increase or decrease, because what is added to what is mentioned would be a part, but the mentioned [parts] are already the sum of the parts, so it would not be a mention of an increase to the definition. From this it is clear that a reality has only one definition, and that the ignorant of the definition is ignorant of the defined, and the knower of it is knower of the defined necessarily. As for the second: because it is a mention of the properties and external attributes of the thing, and that is subject to increase and decrease. In the comparison between definitions and descriptions: the definition is more complete than the description in two ways: ... as for the description, it is mentioned all at once correctly, but it is possible that there are a hundred descriptions; rather it is intended as a description insofar as it is a description. First, because it gives a mental picture conforming to the thing itself, while the description does not have that. As for the second division: because the external attribute does not yield knowledge of the thing unless it is specific to it, but the highest attribute is dependent on knowledge of it; then knowledge of it from that attribute leads to circularity. And as for the indications of that attribute not being specific to others, because it would require confinement [to distinguish it] from all things different from it, and the different things are infinite, which leads to knowledge dependent on infinite knowledge, which is impossible. And as for the description, it is a name from among the simple predicated descriptions, definitively not [a hadd].
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage compares hadd and rasm in terms of completeness and distinctness. The author argues that hadd is inherently complete because it lists all essential parts; any addition would be redundant, and any omission would invalidate it. Consequently, each reality has only one correct hadd. The rasm, being based on accidents, can have many variants, making it less precise. The author then explains two advantages of hadd: (1) it provides a true mental representation matching the reality, whereas rasm does not guarantee this; (2) rasm risks circularity (if the defining attribute itself requires prior knowledge of the defined) or infinite regress (if it attempts to distinguish from all other things by listing differences). An example from fiqh: the hadd of 'salah' might be 'specific statements and actions, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'—this is complete. A rasm might be 'an act of worship that prevents indecency'—this is true but not exhaustive, and other acts may also prevent indecency.

Key Terms
الزيادة
النقصان
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why does al-Razi say increase in hadd is impossible?
A) Because any addition would be a part not originally in the essence.
B) Because the essence is simple and cannot receive addition.
C) Because hadd is derived from revelation.
D) Because increase would make the definition unclear.

Key Takeaway
A complete definition (hadd) cannot be increased or decreased because it already contains all essential parts.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Hadd is Unique, Rasm is Not
Al-Razi demonstrates that hadd is unique and more complete than rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a hadd and a rasm, and explain why a hadd is unique while many rusum are possible.

Core Idea
Each reality has only one correct definition (hadd) but countless descriptions (rusum).

In this lesson, we learn that a true essence admits only one complete definition, while external attributes allow multiple descriptions. Al-Razi also explains why hadd is superior to rasm in giving perfect knowledge, and how rasm can lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about uniqueness
Find where al-Razi says 'الماهية لها حد واحد' (a reality has only one definition). This is a key conclusion from the impossibility of increase or decrease.
2
Understand the reasoning
Because hadd captures the essence itself, which is one thing. Rasm captures accidental properties, which can vary. Thus, many rusum can exist for the same thing.
3
Compare completeness
Al-Razi notes that hadd gives a mental picture conforming to the thing itself, while rasm does not guarantee that. This is the first way hadd is more complete.
4
Examine the pitfalls of rasm
The passage then explains two problems with rasm: (a) circularity – if the defining attribute itself requires knowledge of the thing, defining by it is circular; (b) infinite regress – if the attribute is not specific, one would need to distinguish from infinite things, which is impossible.

This is if the definition is limited. But if it is not, then [the definition] refers to a specific existent and claims that its reality is composed of such-and-such. Then it must have evidence. Increase in hadd is impossible, but in rasm it is possible. As for the first: because it is an expression of the sum of the parts, and that is not subject to increase or decrease, because what is added to what is mentioned would be a part, but the mentioned [parts] are already the sum of the parts, so it would not be a mention of an increase to the definition. From this it is clear that a reality has only one definition, and that the ignorant of the definition is ignorant of the defined, and the knower of it is knower of the defined necessarily. As for the second: because it is a mention of the properties and external attributes of the thing, and that is subject to increase and decrease. In the comparison between definitions and descriptions: the definition is more complete than the description in two ways: ... as for the description, it is mentioned all at once correctly, but it is possible that there are a hundred descriptions; rather it is intended as a description insofar as it is a description. First, because it gives a mental picture conforming to the thing itself, while the description does not have that. As for the second division: because the external attribute does not yield knowledge of the thing unless it is specific to it, but the highest attribute is dependent on knowledge of it; then knowledge of it from that attribute leads to circularity. And as for the indications of that attribute not being specific to others, because it would require confinement [to distinguish it] from all things different from it, and the different things are infinite, which leads to knowledge dependent on infinite knowledge, which is impossible. And as for the description, it is a name from among the simple predicated descriptions, definitively not [a hadd].
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage compares hadd and rasm in terms of completeness and distinctness. The author argues that hadd is inherently complete because it lists all essential parts; any addition would be redundant, and any omission would invalidate it. Consequently, each reality has only one correct hadd. The rasm, being based on accidents, can have many variants, making it less precise. The author then explains two advantages of hadd: (1) it provides a true mental representation matching the reality, whereas rasm does not guarantee this; (2) rasm risks circularity (if the defining attribute itself requires prior knowledge of the defined) or infinite regress (if it attempts to distinguish from all other things by listing differences). An example from fiqh: the hadd of 'salah' might be 'specific statements and actions, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'—this is complete. A rasm might be 'an act of worship that prevents indecency'—this is true but not exhaustive, and other acts may also prevent indecency.

الحد
الزيادة
النقصان

Key Takeaway
A reality has only one hadd, while many rusum are possible; hadd yields certain knowledge, while rasm risks circularity or infinite regress.

Why Naqd and Mu'arada Do Not Invalidate Definitions
Author refutes the claim that definitions can be invalidated by naqd and mu'arada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why objections by counterexample (naqd) and alternative definition (mu'arada) are not valid against a real definition (hadd).

Core Idea
A definition points to an essence without judgment; no judgment means no refutation.

In this lesson, we examine a key debate in Islamic logic: can a definition be refuted by producing a counterexample or by offering a different definition? Al-Razi argues that since a true definition (hadd) is a pure conceptualization (tasawwur) of an essence, it contains no propositional claim (da'wa). Hence, it is immune to naqd and mu'arada. We will also see his classification of errors in definitions into verbal and meaning-based types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about definitions
Notice that al-Razi states a definition (hadd) contains no claims (da'awi) – it merely points to an essence without affirming or denying anything. This is crucial for understanding why refutation is impossible.
2
Explain why naqd fails against hadd
Naqd (counterexample) requires a proposition to contradict. Since hadd is not a proposition, there is nothing to refute. Al-Razi explicitly says 'without assent (tasdiq) to the existence of the defined... the refutation is not valid.'
3
Explain why mu'arada is not damaging
Mu'arada (offering an alternative definition) points to the same essence from a different perspective. It does not negate the original definition because the essence itself remains unchanged.
4
Distinguish verbal vs. meaning-based errors
Al-Razi categorizes problems in definitions: verbal (ambiguous, metaphorical, obscure words) and meaning-based (using something equally obscure, etc.). These are real issues but do not amount to invalidation by naqd or mu'arada.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'substance (jawhar)' as 'something that exists not in a subject'. If someone objects with a different definition like 'something that occupies space', al-Razi would say this does not invalidate the first definition because it points to the same reality differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Knowledge of it... and compound things may have descriptions, and sometimes they cannot be distinguished except by descriptions also. Relations and attributes cannot be defined except by descriptions, because they cannot be defined except by their efficient or receptive causes, and causes are external to the effects; so defining an effect by its cause is a descriptive definition. There is a discussion in definitions and descriptions: some people denied that because the definition, if it does not contain any claims (da'awi), it is simply a reference to the specific essence that the mind points to, without any judgment of negation or affirmation, and that is not subject to proof or refutation. And the statement of whoever says that the definition is invalidated by naqd (counterexample) and mu'arada (counter-argument) is a mistake, because without assent (tasdiq) to the existence of the defined in the place of the definition, or the opposite, the refutation is not valid. And as for mu'arada, it is not damaging because the reality pointed to by the mu'arada, insofar as it is the same reality, does not negate the mentioned reality. However, from the side of the claims that turn into the possibility of objecting to definitions, they said that the problems in definitions are either verbal (lafziyya) – which are that the expressions are homonymous or metaphorical, shifted from common usage, or strange and obscure – or meaning-based (ma'nawiyya), which are either common between definitions and descriptions or specific to each. As for the first [type], it is that the definition [is] either clearer than the definiendum, or more obscure, or using something by which the definiendum is not known. And some of them said that 'qut' [؟] itself enters into the subtle problems, and unless we know, it remains hidden. And the one who says that these aspects are not intelligible in defining essences, because the definiendum must be thought of before its definition.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage addresses objections to definitions. The author first notes that some deny that definitions can be critiqued, since a definition merely points to an essence without affirming or denying anything—hence it cannot be proven or refuted. Criticisms like naqd (finding a counterexample) and mu'arada (offering an alternative definition) are invalid because they require assenting to some proposition about the defined thing, whereas hadd is purely conceptual. The passage then categorizes the types of errors that can occur in definitions into verbal (lafziyya) and meaning-based (ma'nawiyya). Verbal errors include ambiguous, metaphorical, or obscure words. Meaning-based errors include using something equally or more obscure to define, or defining by something not proper to the thing. The author ultimately argues that these issues are not real problems for essential definitions because the definiendum must be conceptualized prior to the definition anyway. An example from kalam: defining 'substance (jawhar)' as 'something that exists not in a subject'—this is a hadd; if someone objects with a different definition (mu'arada), it does not invalidate the first unless evidence is given.

المعارضة

C) Because naqd is only for syllogisms.
D) Because definitions are based on authority.

Key Takeaway
A true definition (hadd) is immune to naqd and mu'arada because it is a conception, not a judgment.

Why Naqd and Mu'arada Do Not Invalidate Definitions
Refutation of naqd and mu'arada as absolute invalidators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which naqd (counterexample) and mu'arada (alternative definition) can or cannot refute a definition, and apply this to a juristic example.

Core Idea
A definition that merely points to an essence is immune to refutation.

The author addresses a common mistake: thinking that any counterexample or alternative definition automatically invalidates a definition. He clarifies that if a definition simply indicates an essence without making any claim, it cannot be refuted. This insight protects legitimate definitions in Islamic sciences from baseless attacks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement that 'the definition is invalidated by naqd and mu'arada'. This is the position the author rejects.
The author calls this statement an 'error' (خطأ).
2
Understand the author's condition
The author explains that a definition without an assertion (دعوى) does not allow affirmation or negation. Only if the definition includes an assertion can naqd or mu'arada apply.
Think of a definition purely as pointing to an essence, not as a proposition.
3
Analyse the reason for mu'arada's harmlessness
Mu'arada presents an alternative definition, but the alternative does not negate the original reality. It is a different description, not a refutation.
Unless the alternative definition is a 'strong counterexample' that changes the possibility of objecting, as the author concedes.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a jurist defining 'riba' as 'excess in exchange of same commodities'. A critic says: 'This definition fails because it includes barter.' Use the author's criteria to evaluate whether this is a valid naqd.
If the definition is merely ostensive (pointing to the essence), barter may not be a counterexample. If it includes an assertion that 'all excess is riba', then a counterexample might work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Because if a definition does not include any assertion, its result returns to indicating the specific essence to which the mind is directed without any judgment of affirmation or negation at all, and that is not subject to affirmation or refutation. The statement of whoever says that the definition is invalidated by naqd and mu'arada is an error, because if not for the assent to the existence of the defined when the definition is given or conversely, and the pain is directed towards the story? And mu'arada is not harmful, because the reality indicated in the mu'arada in terms of that reality does not produce the mentioned reality, or because it is as it is. Unless it is from the strong counterexamples that turn the possibility of objecting to definitions. They said that the defects in it are either verbal, meaning the words are borrowed, metaphorical, or unfamiliar and strange, or conceptual, meaning they are common between definitions and descriptions specific to each one of them.

Commentary
This passage refutes two major objections against definitions and classifies possible defects. The author argues that if a definition merely points to an essence without any judgment (positive or negative), it cannot be refuted by counterexamples (naqd) because naqd presupposes assent to the existence of the defined. Mu'arada (presenting an alternative definition) is also harmless because the alternative definition does not negate the original reality. However, the author concedes that some objections are valid, and these can be divided into verbal defects (lafzhiyya) – such as using borrowed, metaphorical, or strange words – and conceptual defects (ma'nawiyya) that are common to both definitions and descriptions. Classifying Defects in Definitions: Verbal vs Conceptual
Classification of defects as verbal or conceptual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given defect in a definition as either verbal (lafzhi) or conceptual (ma'nawi) based on Razi's classification.

Core Idea
Defects in definitions are either in the wording or in the meaning itself.

After arguing that pure definitions are immune to naqd and mu'arada, the author acknowledges that definitions can still have defects. He divides these defects into two types: those related to the words used (verbal) and those related to the meaning (conceptual). This classification helps in systematically evaluating definitions used in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two categories
The author says defects in definitions are either verbal (lafzhiyya) or conceptual (ma'nawiyya). Verbal defects involve borrowed, metaphorical, or strange words. Conceptual defects are common to both definitions and descriptions.
The Arabic terms: القوادح اللفظية and القوادح المعنوية.
2
Identify verbal defects
Verbal defects include: isti'ara (metaphor), majaz (figurative language), and gharaaba (strangeness/unfamiliar words). These make the definition unclear.
A definition should use clear, literal, and familiar words.
3
Identify conceptual defects
Conceptual defects are issues in the meaning itself, such as being too broad (includes non-instances) or too narrow (excludes instances). The author notes these are common to both hadd and rasm.
These defects affect the logical correctness, not just clarity.
4
Apply to an example
Take the definition: 'Man is a rational animal.' 'Rational animal' is not metaphorical (verbal). But if we defined man as 'the mortal animal', that includes horses, so it is too broad (conceptual defect).
Practice with Islamic examples: define 'kalam' as 'the speech of Allah' – potential verbal defect if 'speech' is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Five Defects of Definitions and One Defect of Descriptions
Enumerates five hadd defects and one rasm defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five specific defects of a proper definition (hadd) and the single defect of a description (rasm) in logical works.

Core Idea
Five defects corrupt a definition; the key is correct proximate genus and differentia in proper order.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhas enumerates the precise ways a definition (hadd) can fail and how a description (rasm) can be defective. Understanding these defects is crucial for evaluating whether a term is properly defined — a skill directly needed in fiqh, usul, and kalam where definitions determine rulings and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hadd from rasm
Recognize that a proper definition (hadd) must consist of a proximate genus and a differentia, while a description (rasm) uses accidents or properties. This distinction underlies the different defects.
2
List the five defects of hadd
Identify each defect: (1) what is placed as genus is not truly a genus, (2) what is placed as differentia is not truly a differentia, (3) the genus is remote instead of proximate, (4) the differentia is remote instead of proximate, (5) the proximate genus and differentia are correct but the differentia is placed before the genus.
These defects are mutually exclusive and cover all ways a hadd can fail.
3
Understand the ordering principle
The genus must be more general and more known than the differentia; thus it should precede the differentia. Reversing this order makes the definition obscure.
4
Identify the single defect of rasm
A description (rasm) is defective if it is not more known (more evident) than the thing described, because the purpose of a description is to clarify by using better-known attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما الامور المختصة بالحدود فلا يمكن الا احد امور خمسة: فان يكون المذكور في مقام الجنس جنسا لا، او لايكون المذكور في مقام الفصلفصلا، او ان كان جنسا لكنه لايكون جنسا قريبا، او ان كان فصلا لكنه لايكون فصلا قريبا، او ان كان الذكر جنسا قريبا وفصلا قريبا لكنه قدم الفصل على الجنس وهو غير جائز لان الجنس اعم من الفصل فيكون اعرف منه والاعرف مقدم على ماليس كذلك. واما الامور المختصة بالرسوم فعنوان لا تكون التهم اعرف من المرسوم.
As for the matters specific to definitions, they cannot be other than one of five matters: that what is mentioned in the place of genus is not a genus, or what is mentioned in the place of differentia is not a differentia, or if it is a genus but it is not a proximate genus, or if it is a differentia but it is not a proximate differentia, or if the mention is of a proximate genus and a proximate differentia but he places the differentia before the genus, which is not permissible because the genus is more general than the differentia, so it is more known, and the more known should be placed before what is not like that. As for the matters specific to descriptions, it is that the description is not more known than the described.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five specific defects (quwādiḥ) that can affect a proper definition (hadd) and one defect specific to a description (rasm). For hadd, the defects are: (1) using a term that is not a genus in the position of genus, (2) using a term that is not a differentia in the position of differentia, (3) using a genus that is not proximate (i.e., a remote genus), (4) using a differentia that is not proximate, and (5) using correct proximate genus and differentia but placing the differentia before the genus, which violates the principle that the more general (genus) is more known and should precede the less general (differentia). For rasm, the sole defect is that the description is not better known (more evident) than the thing described, which defeats the purpose of clarification. These criteria are essential for evaluating whether a definition or description is sound in logical practice.

Key Terms
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)
الجنس القريب (al-jins al-qarīb)
الفصل القريب (al-faṣl al-qarīb)

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five specific defects of a definition (hadd)?
A) The differentia is placed before the genus.
B) The genus mentioned is a remote genus instead of proximate.
C) The definition includes a metaphorical term.
D) The differentia mentioned is not a true differentia.

Key Takeaway
A definition (hadd) can fail in exactly five ways—wrong genus, wrong differentia, remote genus, remote differentia, or reversed order—while a description (rasm) fails if it is not more known than the described.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage.' Identify which of the five defects, if any, apply. (Hint: Is 'beverage' the proximate genus? Is 'intoxicating' a true differentia? Is the order correct?)
?
Examine the description: 'Qiyas is extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause.' Is this a hadd or rasm? If rasm, is it defective according to the passage? Why or why not?

The Five Defects of Definitions and the Debate Over Their Difficulty
Author lists five defects specific to hadd and responds to al-Mu'tabar's objection on ease of definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five defects specific to a proper hadd (definition) and evaluate the objection raised by al-Mu'tabar regarding the ease of definitions.

Core Idea
A definition fails if its genus or differentia is misidentified or misplaced, but its difficulty depends on the aim.

In this passage, the author presents the conditions that make a definition valid, listing five specific defects that can occur in a proper hadd. He then addresses a controversy: is composing definitions inherently difficult, or easy? The author of al-Mu'tabar argued that definitions are easy because they are of names, but the author distinguishes between clarifying names and knowing real essences. By working through this passage, you will learn to spot errors in definitions and understand a key debate in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five defects of hadd
Read the passage and list the five specific problems that can occur when constructing a definition. These all involve errors in the genus or differentia.
The fifth defect is about the order of genus and differentia.
2
Distinguish defects of hadd from conditions of rasm
Note that the five defects are exclusive to hadd. For rasm (description), the passage mentions a separate condition: the defining term must be better known than the defined.
A rasm that fails this condition is called a 'rasm invalid'.
3
Analyze al-Mu'tabar's objection
Al-Mu'tabar argues that definitions are easy because they define names, and names correspond to intelligible things whose common and distinctive parts are known. Understand his reasoning.
His objection targets the claim that definitions are difficult.
4
Evaluate the author's resolution
The author concedes that if the goal is to clarify the meaning of a name, definitions are easy. But if the goal is to know the real essence of something existing in itself, it becomes extremely difficult.
This resolution shows the importance of the purpose of definition.
5
Apply to a real definition from Islamic sciences
Take a definition from usul al-fiqh, such as 'riba is an excess in a contract of exchange without a proper countervalue.' Identify its genus (contract of exchange) and differentia (excess without countervalue), and check if any of the five defects apply.
This reinforces the practical relevance of the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Likewise, the definition (hadd) from the essence is impossible, so for the definition by hadd and the definition by rasm to be from those sections, unless one calls... generalizing these defects to the rusum. As for matters specific to hudud, they are not possible except one of five things: either what is mentioned in the place of genus is not a genus, or what is mentioned in the place of differentia is not a differentia, or if it was a genus but it is not a genus, or if it was a differentia but it is not a differentia, or if the mention is a remote genus and a remote differentia but the differentia is placed before the genus, which is not permissible because the genus is more general than the differentia and thus better known, and what is better known should precede what is not. As for matters specific to rusum, they require that the more general be not better known than the defined. The objector who disapproves this says that if the defined is known before the rasm, then the rasm is clearer or better known, but if that were so, knowledge of the defined would not be derived from the rasm, so the rasm would not be a rasm. Then he said: The difficulty of composing hudud is due to the difficulty of knowing the proximate genus and the proximate differentia according to his treatment. When the Shaykh singled that out, the author of al-Mu'tabar objected to him, saying: That is extremely easy, because hudud are definitions of names, and names are names of intelligible things, and every intelligible thing must be understood as to what is its common part and what is its completeness part, so the hadd is easy from this aspect. The fair judgment is that if the purpose was to detail the meaning of the name, the matter is as the author of al-Mu'tabar said; but if the aim was to know the essences existing in themselves, that is extremely difficult. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for proper definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). It first addresses a preceding point about definitions being impossible if the definable shares exact genus/species? Then it lists five specific defects that can occur in a proper hadd: (1) the term mentioned as a genus is not actually a genus; (2) the term mentioned as a differentia is not a differentia; (3) a term is a genus but fails to function as such; (4) a term is a differentia but fails; (5) the order is reversed by placing the narrower differentia before the wider genus. Then it discusses rusum: the defining term (al-a'amm) must be better known than the defined. An objector argues that if the defined is already known, the rasm is redundant. The author then notes that the difficulty of composing definitions stems from the difficulty of identifying the correct proximate genus and differentia. The author of al-Mu'tabar objected, arguing that definitions are of names and thus easy, as we already know the common and distinctive parts of any intelligible concept. The response: this is true if definitions are for clarifying names, but if the aim is to know real essences, it is extremely difficult. The passage ends with 'And with Allah is success.'

C) The definition includes an accident instead of an essential property.
D) The differentia is placed before the genus.

Key Takeaway
Proper definitions require correct identification of proximate genus and differentia, and the difficulty varies based on whether we seek verbal or real definitions.

The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author defines three types of disjunctive conditionals and their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real (haqiqiyya), exclusive (mani'a al-jam'), and inclusive (mani'a al-khuluw) disjunctions and identify their key properties.

Core Idea
A disjunction's logical type depends on whether it excludes conjunction, emptiness, or both.

In this passage, al-Razi clarifies the classification of disjunctive conditionals, a key tool for accurate reasoning in Islamic sciences. He distinguishes three types based on what they exclude: real disjunctions exclude both conjunction and emptiness, exclusive disjunctions exclude only conjunction, and inclusive disjunctions exclude only emptiness. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for constructing valid inferences in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunction mentioned
Look for the terms: haqiqiyya, mani'a al-jam', mani'a al-khuluw. Note that the real disjunction is also called 'haqiqiyya'.
These terms are defined in the passage.
2
Examine the number of parts allowed for each
The real disjunction has exactly two parts. The exclusive can have infinitely many parts. The inclusive cannot have more than two.
The author gives examples of multi-part exclusives like listing all shapes under a polygon.
3
Check the logical relation between parts
In a real disjunction, one part true implies the other false, and one false implies the other true (exhaustive and exclusive). In an exclusive, both can be false but not both true. In an inclusive, both can be true but not both false.
4
Apply the property of the necessary consequent
In a real disjunction, the contradictory of one part necessarily takes the place of the other. For example, if you negate one disjunct, you affirm the other.
This property allows modus tollendo ponens inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ناقصا أو مساويا وقولنا: أما واسعة أن يكون هذا العدد من اللون وجالونوج الزوج أو روج الفداء مركز زوج الزوج والفرد وقد تكون ذات أجناد عن تناهية كقولنا: هذا المضلع كالشم لطارف يكون مثلنا لومربعا وهلم جرا. Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three types of disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) in logic. The author begins by discussing the real disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) which inherently has exactly two mutually exclusive parts. He notes that although a disjunction may appear to have more than two parts, such as in the example 'either it is a triangle or a square or a pentagon', upon analysis these are actually composed of multiple binary disjunctions. The real disjunction is strictly bipartite. Next, he treats the exclusive disjunctive (al-munfasila al-mani'a al-jam'), which excludes conjunction but not emptiness; this type can be composed of an infinite number of parts because it essentially lists all particulars under a genus, with no part being more appropriate than another. In contrast, the inclusive disjunctive (al-mani'a al-khuluw) excludes emptiness but allows conjunction, and it cannot have more than two parts because it involves a correlate and its opposite. The author then explains a property of disjunctives: in a real disjunction, the contradictory of one part necessarily takes the place of the other part. That necessary consequent may be either negative or affirmative. He illustrates with examples involving 'being in the sea' and 'drowning', but the OCR is too garbled to extract precise examples. The passage emphasizes the distinction between the types based on the number of parts and the logical relation between them.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
اللازم

B) The exclusive disjunctive (al-mani'a al-jam')
C) The inclusive disjunctive (al-mani'a al-khuluw)
D) All disjunctions

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals come in three types: real (mutually exclusive and exhaustive), exclusive (cannot both be true but can both be false), and inclusive (cannot both be false but can both be true).
