Against Existence as an Accident: The Necessary Being
Author argues against existence as accidental, defends simplicity of Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's arguments against existence being an accidental attribute and explain why the Necessary Being must be simple.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence cannot be an accident; it must be identical to its essence.

This passage grapples with a foundational debate in Islamic philosophy: is existence (wujud) an accidental property added to essence, or is it identical to essence? Focusing on the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud), the author argues that if existence were an accident, it would lead to contradictions and infinite regress. This lesson will guide you through his key reductio arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target position
Find where the author states the position he is attacking: that existence is an accidental attribute added to the essence of possible beings.
Look for phrases like 'if existence were an accident of possible essences'.
2
Extract the reductio arguments
List the absurd consequences the author derives from the accidentality view, such as: conversion of accident into substance, infinite regress of differentiae, and composition of the Necessary Being.
These are found in the text after the conditional statements.
3
Understand the alternative
The author concludes that for the Necessary Being, existence must be identical to its essence, making it simple and not composite. This avoids the contradictions.
The term 'simple' (basit) is often used for God.
4
Connect to metaphysical context
Relate this argument to the broader debate: Avicenna held existence as accident; later thinkers like Suhrawardi and Mulla Sadra critiqued this. This passage appears to align with the critique.
This helps situate the lesson within Islamic intellectual history.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

النساني الوجودهر نفس حقیقته او لاندك عليه خمسة براهين المانه اعلم فا وجوده اما ان يكون مساوحًا لوجود المكنات في كونه وجودل او لا يكون سرخ بیان والثاني من افتاده و الاول الانخل و اما ان يكون وجوده مقادقًا لماهية اشتراك وغيره او لايكون والثانى باطل لان الوجو علما هو هو لما ان يقضى ان يكون مقاد خا لماهية أو يتمنى ان لا يكون مقارن الماهية اولا يقضى واحدا منهما فان كان الأول موجود واجب الوجود مقارن للماهية وقد فرض انه كبير كذلك من الخلف وإن كان الثاني موجود المكنات غير مقارب الشيء من الماهيات هذ التصرف بالاتفاق ولان المكنات موجودة موجودها هو نفسنا حياتها اندهان ماهیا تھا متخالفه فعى نے وجود لتها الباله قطر واما الرائع فل واحد المشير كاس جميع الموهوداب لک لزم ان لا يكون الوجود مفهوما از نکور مخالفة فی وجود انها و لوکا 2 ما هنا ننا مطالعہ ترم ان لوگان می ماهنابها زاند علی منجھا تھا ولان الما هنة العلة قسم التخرج عن بو حملہ مستحصلہ صبح تكون مفرومه باطلا او اجماع المصير أو استعارہ بنا و عروه عالم شت کا بین جميع الموجودات نوم الملازم فلان الوهو مالم واكون وجود الممكنات نفس ماهيتها
You stayed that the differentia is for the attainment of the species in the genus, and if so, it would necessitate... existence is internal... and it is a proof... and it would be one... from the distant existing things, the constitutive is composed of one... existence, its existence is external, specific... such that it is common. If existence were an accident of possible essences subsumed under it, it would necessitate either the conversion of accident into substance or the conversion of substance into accident in existence. Either it is the subject or predicated... for if it is a subject, it would be substance, because whenever it exists externally, it has no subject, and existence is part of the subject. We speak on this... and the species, and if it were, it would necessitate... and the first name, if it were the subject, it would be an accident... and if it were, it would necessitate... thus it is impossible. Therefore it is necessary to reject... the annihilation of the differentia. Likewise, the common essentials would be a genus, and the species under it are distinguished by existing differentiae, so the genus enters the nature of the differentia. Its separation from the species requires another differentia ad infinitum. And because the differentia is the cause of its existence, so the existent would have another existence, and this is not [valid]. The Master said: Because the distinction of the Necessary from the possible is by a constitutive differentia, so the Necessary would be composite, due to the potentiality of the constitutive for the things subsumed under it. In itself, if it is independent of subject, it is substance, and if it is dependent, it is accident. This is a contradiction. If it is not... the existence of the possible is identical to its essence or not. There are five proofs for it. Know that its existence either is equal to the existence of possibles in being existence or not. The second is false, the first is false. And its existence either corresponds to the essence in sharing and other, or not. The second is false because existence... it requires that it correspond to the essence or not correspond, or it does not require either. If the first, then the Necessary Existent corresponds to essence, and it is assumed that it is otherwise, contradiction. If the second, the existents of possibles are not corresponding to any essence, this is absurd by agreement, because possibles exist, their existence is their essences themselves, their essences are different, so their existences are different, contradiction. If the third, existence is not characterized by either of these two conditions, so existence is separated from essences by a cause, so the Necessary Existent in itself would be possible in itself, contradiction. And another: the one existence itself either signifies itself, or it is not inside it, or an outside matter. The first two... all existence... if it were necessary in itself... you see its existence is upon... to its knowledge detailed... and if the particular Necessary were... its [؟] and the [؟] is one... all existents would lack the concept of existence due to no difference in their existences. And if existence of possibles is identical to their essences, [then they would be necessary].
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments concerning the nature of existence (wujud) and the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). The author begins by discussing the differentia (fasl) and its role in constituting species within a genus. He then moves to the main argument: if existence were an accidental attribute added to the essences of possible beings, then either existence would become a substance (if it is a subject) or the essence would become an accident (if existence is predicated). Both lead to contradiction. He then argues that the differentia cannot be separated from the species without infinite regress, and that if the differentia is the cause of existence, the existent would require another existence. Citing 'the Master' (likely Ibn Sina or a Peripatetic philosopher), he states that if the distinction between the Necessary and the possible is by a constitutive differentia, the Necessary would be composite. The passage continues with five proofs (barahin) regarding existence: first, whether the existence of the Necessary is like that of possibles; second, whether existence corresponds to essence. Both alternatives are shown to be false or lead to absurdities. The author concludes that existence cannot be merely an accident, and that the Necessary Being's existence must be identical to its essence. Otherwise, infinite regress and composition arise. The arguments are typical of the metaphysical debate on the nature of existence in Islamic philosophy, particularly in Avicennan and post-Avicennan thought.

Key Terms
الوجود
واجب الوجود

C) Essence becomes unnecessary
D) Existence becomes substance

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's existence must be identical to its essence to avoid composition and infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a reductio argument: Suppose existence is a property added to the essence of a possible being. Show that this would make the possible being composed of essence and existence, leading to an infinite regress if each component also requires existence. This is similar to the author's argument about the differentia causing another existence.

The Definition of Essence and Its Neutrality Regarding Accidents
Opening of the second chapter on essence and its accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'essence' (mahiyyah) and explain why oneness is not part of the essence itself using the example of horseness.

Core Idea
Essence is what makes a thing itself; its attributes like unity are added from outside.

The second chapter of our text opens with a crucial distinction: every thing has a reality (haqiqah) that makes it what it is, and this reality is distinct from everything else. This is the foundation for understanding the five universals and the difference between essence and accident. The author will use the example of 'horseness' to show that oneness—an accidental attribute—is not included in the definition of the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of essence
Find in the passage where the author states that every thing has a reality by which it is what it is, and that reality is distinct from everything else. This is the definition of mahiyyah (essence).
The Arabic term 'haqiqah' is here synonymous with mahiyyah.
2
Examine the example of horseness
The author says horseness insofar as it is horseness is neither one nor not-one. This means that the essence of horseness, considered in itself, does not include the attribute of unity. Oneness is added from outside.
The word 'horseness' (al-fursiyyah) is a technical example for any quiddity.
3
Understand why oneness is accidental
Oneness (al-wahidiyyah) is an attribute that can attach to an essence, but it is not part of the essence's definition. For example, a horse is one, but 'being one' is not part of what makes a horse a horse.
This shows that accidents are external to the essence.
4
Apply to the humanity example
The passage mentions that humanity in Zayd and humanity in 'Amr are not numerically one even though they share the same essence. This is because our consideration of 'humanity' drops all individualizing accidents.
Thus, the essence considered without accidents is one in definition but multiple in individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الماهية عن لواحقها ان لكل شيء حقيقة هو بها هو وهو مغاير لجميع ما عداه لائحة كانت المفارقة فالفرنسية من حيث هي فرنسية لا واحدة ولا لا واحدة على ان تكون الواحداها داخلة في مفهوم مقابل الواحدية صفة خضمة اليها فيكون الفرنسية معها واحدة واللا واحدة ايضا لمرحضموم اليها في كون الفرنسية معهما لا واحدة والفرسة من حيث فى فرنسية ليت الا الفرنسية فان النا) هل الفرسُ الف اولين قلت ليش الفرس من حيث هو فرس الف ولا نقول القرن من حيث مو فرس ليس القامات قبل الأنسانية التي في زيد الغابر التي في عمرو من حيث فى إنسانية فلا يلزم من ذلك ان نقوك فاذن ذلك وهى واحدة بالعدد لأن قولنا من حيث إنسانية اسقط عنها كل ما عداها من الاعتبارات والولة اعتبار ذاتك عليها موجب حذفها واعلم انه من ان احيوان لا بشه شي
The second chapter on essence and its accidents. That every thing has a reality by which it is what it is, and it is distinct from everything else. For example, horseness insofar as it is horseness is neither one nor not one, such that oneness enters into the concept in opposition to oneness as an attribute added to it, so horseness with it is one, and not-one is also added to it, so horseness with them is not one. And horseness insofar as it is horseness is nothing but horseness. For example, do you say 'horse'? I say: horse insofar as it is horse is 'horse', and we do not say 'horse insofar as it is horse is a hundred'. The humanities that are in Zayd and those that are in 'Amr insofar as they are humanity are not necessarily such that we say they are one in number, because our statement 'insofar as they are humanity' drops off all other considerations, and the consideration of its essence necessarily drops them. Know that from [the fact that] animal is not a thing...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the second chapter, defining the essence (mahiyyah) of a thing. The author states that every thing has a reality (haqiqah) by which it is what it is, and that reality is distinct from everything else. He then discusses the properties of essence, such as universality and particularity. The text seems to use the example of 'horseness' (al-fursiyyah) to illustrate that essence, considered in itself, is neither one nor many; unity is an external attribute. Similarly, humanity in Zayd and humanity in 'Amr are not numerically one because the essence considered without accidents is not subject to number. The passage is heavily corrupted, but the main idea is that essence, in itself, is neutral with respect to further determinations.

D) It is always black.

Key Takeaway
The essence of a thing is its reality that makes it what it is, and it is neutral to all accidents, including oneness.

Essence and Its Properties: Simple and Composite
Author defines essence, discusses its properties, and divides it into simple and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define essence (mahiyya) and distinguish between its simple and composite forms, and explain why essence qua essence is independent of external attributes like oneness.

Core Idea
Essence is the reality of a thing, independent of attributes like oneness.

In this passage, al-Razi defines essence as the reality by which a thing is what it is, distinct from everything else. He explains that essence qua essence is neither one nor not one—oneness is an external attribute. He then argues against the idea that essences exist abstractly in the mind, and divides essences into simple and composite to avoid infinite regress. Understanding this foundation is crucial for later discussions in kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of essence
Find the Arabic phrase where the author defines essence. Look for 'ماهية' and the statement that it is the reality by which a thing is what it is.
The definition emphasizes distinctness from all other things.
2
Understand the independence of essence from attributes
Note that al-Razi says essence qua essence is not one nor not one; oneness is added externally. This shows that essence itself has no inherent multiplicity or unity.
The example of humanity in Zayd and 'Amr illustrates that humanity qua humanity is not numerically one.
3
Examine the argument against abstract essences
Follow the reasoning: 'animal' is not existent by itself because it is a part. Essence is not found abstract at all; considering it as such is different from its existence. This refutes the famous premise that essences are abstract in the mind.
The author uses the example of 'animal' to show part-whole relationship.
4
Differentiate simple and composite essences
Composite essences are understood from a combination of several realities (e.g., body as matter+form). Simple essences are not. The author argues simple essences must exist to avoid infinite regress.
The body example: self-subsistent, composite of matter (hayula) and form (surah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الماهية ولواحقها ان لكل شي حقيقة هو بها هو وهو مغاير لجميع ما عداه الماهية من حيث هي ماهية لا واحدة ولا لا واحدة على ان تكون الواحدة داخلة في مفهومها مقابل الواحدية صفة خارجة عنها فيكون الماهية معها واحدة واللا واحدة ايضا امر مضوم اليها في كون الماهية معهما لا واحدة والماهية من حيث هي ماهية ليست الا الماهية فان النا هل الفرس من حيث هو فرس الف ولا نقول الفرس من حيث هو فرس ليس الف لان الانسانية التي في زيد الغابر التي في عمرو من حيث هي انسانية فلا يلزم من ذلك ان نقول هي واحدة بالعدد لان قولنا من حيث انسانية اسقط عنها كل ما عداها من الاعتبارات واعلم ان من ان الحيوان لا بشيء موجود لا نه جزء الموجود في الخارج فيكون موجودا في الخارج وليس حق ان الحيوان بشرط لان موجود اما الاعيان فظاهر واما الذهن فلانا ولو قلنا به لم يكن هناك ايضا مجرد لان كونه في ذلك الذهن من التوافق بل كونه مجردا من اللواحق فالمجرد اذا كان معه قيد التجرد لم يبق مجردا عن اللواحق فالماهية لا توجد مجردة اصلا وان كان اعتبارها من حيث هي هي مغاير لاعتبار وجودها وبهذا يظهر فساد للمقدمة المشهورة من ان الماهيات تكون مجردة في العقل فاقسم الماهيات هذا من وجعين اما ان تكون مركبة واما ان تكون بسيطة والمركبة هي التي انا تعليم حقيقتها من اجتماع عدة حقائق والبسيطة الا كذلك ولا بد من الاعتراف بالبسيطة والا لتسلسل كل ماهية من اخر ولانهاية لها ومع ذلك فلا بد من البسيطة لان الواحد فيها موجود والماهية قد تكون قائمة بنفسها وقد تكون حالة في غيرها والاول اما ان يكون بسيطا او مركبا يكون بعض اجزائه قائما بالنفس ويكون محلا للباقي كالجسم القائم بنفسه فانه مركب من الهيولى والصورة فيكون الهيولى التي في المحل محلا للصورة والثاني ان كان بسيطا فلا كلام فيه وان كان مركبا واعلم ان الوجود ومن الامكان
The second chapter on essence and its appendages. That everything has a reality by which it is what it is, distinct from everything else. Essence qua essence is neither one nor not one, on the basis that 'oneness' is not part of its concept; rather, oneness is an attribute external to it, so essence with oneness is one, and 'not one' is also something added so essence with both is not one. Essence qua essence is nothing but essence. For example, we do not say that the horse qua horse is one, nor do we say the horse qua horse is not one. Because the humanity in Zayd and 'Amr qua humanity does not require saying they are numerically one, because saying 'qua humanity' strips away all other considerations. Know that 'animal' is not existent by itself because it is a part of what exists externally, so it exists externally, but it is not correct that animal is conditioned... If we said that, there would still not be abstraction, because its being in the mind is not abstraction, but rather being abstracted from accidents. So essence is not found abstract at all, although considering it as such is different from considering its existence. By this the falsity of the famous premise that essences are abstract in the mind becomes clear. So the division of essences: from two aspects they are either composite or simple. Composite is that whose reality is understood from the combination of several realities; simple is not. And it is necessary to admit simplicity, otherwise there would be infinite regress because every essence of an accident has no end. Nevertheless, there must be simple, because the one is found in it. Essence may be self-subsistent or inhering in another. The first is either simple or composite such that some parts are self-subsistent and a locus for the rest, like body which is self-subsistent and composite of matter and form. The matter is a locus for the form. The second, if simple then no issue; if composite... Know that existence...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage opens the second chapter on essence (mahiyya). The author first defines essence as the reality by which a thing is what it is, distinct from everything else. He then explains that essence qua essence is neither one nor not one because oneness is a property added to essence. The example of humanity shows that universal qualities are not numerically one across individuals. The author then discusses whether essences exist abstractly, arguing that they do not; the famous claim that essences are abstract in the mind is false. He then divides essences into simple and composite. Composite essences are understood from multiple realities; simple ones are not. He argues that simple essences must exist to avoid infinite regress. He further discusses whether an essence is self-subsistent or inherent, giving the example of body as composite of matter and form.

Key Terms

البسيطة
المركبة
الهيولى

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Essence is the reality of a thing, independent of attributes; it is either simple or composite, and simple essences prevent infinite regress.

Classifying Essence: Self-Subsistent and Accidental
Classification of essence into self-subsistent and accidental, and further subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between self-subsistent and accidental essences and further classify self-subsistent essences into simple and composite.

Core Idea
Essence is either self-subsistent or accidental; if self-subsistent, it is either simple or composite.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas begins by categorising essence (mahiyya) into two fundamental types: that which stands by itself (qā'im bi-dhātihā) and that which inheres in a subject (ḥālla fī maḥall). The former is then divided into simple (basīṭ) and composite (murakkab). This classification is foundational for later discussions on whether a composite requires an external maker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories
Look at the Arabic: 'والماهية قد تكون قائمة بذاتها وقد تكون حالة في محل'. Essence can be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-dhātihā) or accidental (ḥālla fī maḥall).
2
Further division of the self-subsistent
The phrase 'والأول إما أن يكون بسيطا أو مركبا' tells us that the first (self-subsistent) is either simple or composite.
3
Example of a composite
The text gives the example of body: 'كالجسم القائم بالنفسية فإنه مركب من الهيولى والصورة'. Body is composite of matter and form, with self-subsistent parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والماهية قد تكون قائمة بذاتها وقد تكون حالة في محل. والأول إما أن يكون بسيطا أو مركبا. فإن كان مركبا يكون بعض أجزائه قائما بالنفس ويكون محلا للباقي كالجسم القائم بالنفسية فإنه مركب من الهيولى والصورة. وإن كان بسيطا فلا كلام فيه. وإن كان مركبا فهل تساقط أجزائه تكون مجعولة أم لا؟ المحققون يقولون: المركب محتاج إلى كل واحد منها بسيط، وعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء يجب حصول ذلك المركب لا محالة. فإن لم يكن البسيط مجعولا لم يكن المركب الواجب الحصول عند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء المجعولة مجعولا، فتجب نفي المجعولية. والخلف مثال الماهية والوجود. وانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر: كل واحد منها غير مجعول بالذات، فسلام أن لا تكون الماهية الموجودة مجعولة.
And essence (mahiyya) may be self-subsistent or it may be an accident in a subject. The first is either simple or composite. If it is composite, some of its parts are self-subsistent and some are subject for the others, like body which is self-subsistent and is composed of matter and form. If it is simple, then no discussion. If it is composite, are its parts made (maj'ul) or not? The verifiers say: the composite needs each of its parts which is simple, and when those parts come together, the composite necessarily comes to be. If the simple is not made, then the composite which necessarily comes to be from those made parts would not be made, so the denial of being made is necessary. The circularity is exemplified by essence and existence. The attribution of one to the other: each one of them is not made in itself, so it is safe that the existing essence is not made.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a definitional and argumentative discussion about essence (mahiyya). The author first classifies essence into two types: self-subsistent (qā'im bi-dhātihā) and accidental (ḥālla fī maḥall). The self-subsistent is further divided into simple and composite. If composite, like the body composed of matter and form, the parts themselves may be self-subsistent and serve as subjects for others. The author then poses the question: are the parts of a composite made (maj'ūl) or not? The verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) argue that a composite depends on each of its simple parts; when those parts are assembled, the composite necessarily results. If the simple parts themselves are not made (i.e., they exist without a cause), then the composite, which necessarily comes from them, cannot be made either. This leads to a denial of 'being made' for the composite, which is problematic. The example given is that of essence and existence (al-māhiyya wa al-wujūd). Each of these is not made in itself—their mutual attribution (i.e., existence inhering in essence) does not require an external maker for the existing essence. The argument guards against the misconception that a composite entity always requires a separate efficient cause; sometimes the parts themselves suffice.

Key Terms
الماهية
قائمة بذاتها
حالة في محل
بسيط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two primary categories of essence?
A) Simple and composite
B) Self-subsistent and accidental
C) Necessary and contingent
D) Material and immaterial

Key Takeaway
Essence is fundamentally classified as either self-subsistent or accidental; self-subsistent further divides into simple and composite.

Given the definition of a human as a rational animal, classify 'humanity' as self-subsistent or accidental, and then as simple or composite. Explain your reasoning.

The Conditional Argument: If Simple Parts Are Not Made, the Composite Is Not Made
Conditional argument on the making of composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument about the making of composites and apply it to the example of essence and existence.

Core Idea
If the simple parts of a composite are uncaused, the composite itself is uncaused.

After classifying essence, al-Razi presents a conditional argument about composite entities. The verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) argue that a composite depends on its simple parts. If those simple parts are not made (ghayr maj'ūl), then the composite that necessarily results from them cannot be made either. This argument is illustrated by the relationship between essence and existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent
Find the phrase 'فإن لم يكن البسيط مجعولا' which states: 'If the simple is not made (maj'ūl)'. This is the 'if' part of the conditional.
2
Identify the consequent
The text continues: 'لم يكن المركب الواجب الحصول عند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء المجعولة مجعولا' meaning 'then the composite that necessarily comes to be from those made parts would not be made'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The composite necessarily results when its parts are assembled. If the parts are uncaused, the composite cannot be caused by them; it would be uncaused. This is a modus ponens argument if the antecedent is accepted.
4
Apply the example
The example of essence and existence: each is not made in itself, so their composite (existing essence) is also not made. This prevents an infinite regress of causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition of Length (al-Ṭūl)
Author defines the three dimensions of continuous quantity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define length (ṭūl) as a dimension of continuous quantity, identify its differentia (having one part longer than another), and provide Islamic examples from human anatomy and cosmology.

Core Idea
Length is the dimension where one part exceeds another in extension.

In this passage, Razi introduces length as the first of the three dimensions of continuous quantity. Understanding length is crucial for grasping how Islamic scholars measured and described spatial extension. We will see how length is defined by the relation of parts and exemplified by the human body and the universe.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of length
Find the sentence where the author defines length (يراد بالطول الامتداد الذي بعضه أطول من بعضه الآخر).
The definition is relative: one part is longer than another.
2
Identify the examples
The author gives two examples: from the human head to foot, and from the center of the world to its circumference.
These examples show length in different contexts.
3
Distinguish from width and depth
Note that width and depth have different definitions: width intersects two dimensions, depth goes front to back.
Length is unique in that its parts are compared in a single direction.
4
Apply the definition to a new case
Think of a rod: its longest extension is its length. Could the same rod have different length if oriented differently?
Length is relative to the object's orientation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الامتداد الواحد فالطول والعرض والعمق. يراد بالطول الامتداد الذي بعضه أطول من بعضه الآخر، كالامتداد المأخوذ من رأس الإنسان إلى قدمه، والامتداد المأخوذ من مركز العالم إلى محيطه. والعرض يراد به المقدار الذي فيه بعدان، والبعد المقاطع للبعدين المحيطين بالسطح، والمأخوذ من عين الإنسان إلى شماله. والعمق مثلاً الخط إذا فاض ابتداءً... والبعد الذي يعود الإنسان قدامه وخلفه، وفي سائر الحيوانات ما تحويه السطوح فوقه وأسفله. ثم نقول إن أريد بالطول والعرض والعمق نفس الامتدادات فقد كانت خطان... وإن أريد بها سائر المعاني فهي كميات ما حرفة مع إضافات.
As for the single extension, it is length, width, and depth. By length is meant the extension some part of which is longer than another part, like the extension taken from the head of a human to his foot, and the extension taken from the center of the world to its circumference. By width is meant the magnitude that has two dimensions, and the dimension intersecting the two dimensions surrounding the surface, and taken from the eye of a human to his north. By depth, for example, the line when it extends initially... and the dimension that returns to a person in front and behind, and in other animals what the surfaces above and below encompass. Then we say: if by length, width, and depth are meant the extensions themselves, then they were two lines... and if by them are meant other meanings, then they are quantities that are relative with additions.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines the three primary dimensions of continuous quantity: length (ṭūl), width (ʿarḍ), and depth (ʿumq). The author begins by stating that the single extension is these three. He defines length as the dimension where one part is longer than another, giving examples from the human body (head to foot) and cosmology (center of the world to its circumference). Width is defined as a magnitude containing two dimensions, specifically the dimension that cuts across the two dimensions surrounding a surface, exemplified by the direction from the eye to the north. Depth is illustrated with a line that extends, and the dimension that goes front to back. The passage then notes a subtle point: if these terms refer to the extensions themselves, they are considered lines (two dimensions), but if they refer to other meanings (e.g., relative measures), they are quantities with additions. This shows the author's concern with distinguishing between absolute and relative quantification.

Key Terms
الطول
العرض
العمق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The dimension from the eye to the north
B) The dimension from the head to the foot
C) The dimension from front to back
D) The dimension from the center to the north

Key Takeaway
Length is defined by the comparison of parts where one is longer than another, not by absolute size.

Definition of Width (al-ʿArḍ)
Author defines the three dimensions of continuous quantity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define width (ʿarḍ) as a magnitude with two dimensions, intersecting the two dimensions surrounding a surface, and provide the Islamic example from the direction of the eye to the north.

Core Idea
Width is the dimension that cuts across the two dimensions of a surface.

Razi defines width as a more complex dimension than length: it involves two dimensions and intersects the surrounding surface. This lesson unpacks that definition with a concrete example from human anatomy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of width
Find the part where the author defines width (العرض يراد به المقدار الذي فيه بعدان، والبعد المقاطع للبعدين المحيطين بالسطح).
Note that width is a magnitude with two dimensions.
2
Understand the 'two dimensions'
Width is not a single line but involves two dimensions, meaning it has both length and width itself? This is tricky. The author says width is a magnitude that has two dimensions, and it intersects the two dimensions surrounding a surface.
Width is a magnitude that itself has two dimensions (like a plane) but it is the dimension that cuts across the other two.
3
Identify the example
The example given is 'from the eye to the north' (المأخوذ من عين الإنسان إلى شماله). This direction is not purely vertical or horizontal but diagonal? Actually, from the eye to the north is a horizontal direction, but it intersects the other dimensions.
The example illustrates how width is measured.
4
Compare with length and depth
Length had one part longer than another; depth goes front to back; width is the dimension that cuts across.
Each dimension has a distinct relational property.

Definition of Depth (al-ʿUmq)
Author defines the three dimensions of continuous quantity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define depth (ʿumq) as the dimension going front to back and up and down, and provide examples from human and animal anatomy.

Core Idea
Depth is the dimension that extends forward/backward and upward/downward, perpendicular to length and width.

Razi defines depth as the third dimension, perpendicular to both length and width. This lesson focuses on his description and examples.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of depth
Find where the author discusses depth (العمق مثلاً الخط إذا فاض ابتداءً... والبعد الذي يعود الإنسان قدامه وخلفه).
Depth is illustrated by a line that extends and by the dimension in front and behind a person.
2
Understand the examples
The examples are front/back for humans, and above/below for animals. Depth is the dimension perpendicular to both length and width.
Depth is often the vertical dimension when length is horizontal.
3
Distinguish from other dimensions
Length is longer part to another; width cuts across; depth is the perpendicular extension.
These are relative to orientation.
4
Note the line example
Razi says 'the line when it extends initially...' which suggests depth as the dimension of extension away from the surface.
Depth is the dimension that gives volume from a surface.

Why Quantity Lacks an Opposite and Does Not Admit Intensity
Author argues that quantity has no opposite and does not admit intensity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Razi argues that quantity has no opposite and does not admit intensity, using the distinction between discrete and continuous quantities.

Core Idea
Quantity is not a quality: no opposition, no intensity.

Razi presents two key features of the category of quantity (al-kamm): it has no opposite, and it does not admit intensity. Unlike qualities that can be opposed (hot/cold) or intensified (warmer/hotter), quantities like numbers and magnitudes behave differently. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two divisions of quantity
Razi divides quantity into discrete (al-munfaṣil, e.g., number) and continuous (al-muttaṣil, e.g., line). He addresses each separately to show why they lack opposites.
2
Why discrete quantities have no opposite
Numbers are either multiples or divisors of each other; they share no common substrate that could receive opposites. No number is the opposite of another.
3
Why continuous quantities have no opposite
Magnitudes do not truly oppose each other; they differ in size but not in a way that constitutes ontological opposition.
4
Why quantity does not admit intensity
Intensity applies to qualities, not quantities. A longer line is not 'more line-like' but simply larger. The difference is quantitative, not qualitative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان الكم لا ضد له. أما للفصل فلأن كل عدد إما حاصل للعدد الآخر أو مستقوم به ولا ضد لما هو مفتوح أو منقوم به. ولأنه ليس من العددين موضوع قرب مشترك. وأما المتصل فالمقادير منه كل واحد منها لا يقابل الآخر ولا مقبوله فلا تحقق التضاد. وان الكم لا يقبل الاستشداد والنقص. من الظاهر أنه لا عقل أقوى في الثلاثة من ثلاثة أخرى ولا خط أشرف في الخطية من خط آخر وإن كان أطول منه ولكن ذلك لا يكون اشتداداً بل تدبدل في المقدار. وأما اللانهاية فبالحقيقة هو أن يكون ذلك الأمر حاصلاً للشيء لكن النهاية لا تكون حاصلة له إما لكونه بحيث إذا أخذت منه مقداري شيء وجدت شيئاً خارجاً عنه من غير حاجة إلى العود كما يقال الأجسام عند عظم العظم. ولأنه لا طرف بالفعل ولا حد محيط به. وبالمجاز يقال للبعد الذي يكون سلوكه متعددا أو متعسرا.
And that quantity has no opposite. As for the discrete, because every number is either a divisor of another number or a multiple, and there is no opposite to what is open or what is divided. And because there is no common substrate for two numbers. As for the continuous, the magnitudes each one of them does not oppose another and is not received, so opposition is not realized. And that quantity does not admit intensity and diminution. It is apparent that there is no intellect stronger than another intellect in three, nor a line nobler than another line in lineality even if it is longer, but that is not intensity but rather change in magnitude. As for infinity, in reality it is that that thing belongs to the thing but the end does not belong to it, either because it is such that if you take from it a certain amount you find something external to it without need to return, as is said of bodies regarding immense magnitude. And because there is no actual extremity nor encompassing limit. And metaphorically it is said of the distance whose traversal is repeated or difficult.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage discusses three properties of quantity (al-kamm). First, quantity has no opposite (lā ḍidda lahu). The author argues for this by examining the two main divisions: discrete (al-munfaṣil) and continuous (al-muttaṣil). For discrete quantities like numbers, one number cannot be the opposite of another because they are either multiples or divisors, and they share no common substrate that could receive opposites. For continuous quantities like magnitudes, each magnitude does not truly oppose another; they differ in size but not in a way that constitutes ontological opposition. Second, quantity does not admit intensity (lā yaqbal al-ishdidād). Unlike qualities (e.g., heat), a quantity cannot be 'more intense' in itself. A line can be longer but not 'more line-like' than another; a number three is not 'more three-ish' than another three. Differences are of quantity, not quality. Third, the passage defines infinity (al-lā nihāya). True infinity means that no limit can be reached: if you take any portion, there remains something beyond without needing to return. Metaphorical infinity is just a very large or difficult-to-traverse distance. The examples (like bodies) show that physical magnitudes can be immense but not truly infinite.

Key Terms
الكم
الضد
الاستشداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does a number have no opposite?
A) Because numbers are infinite
B) Because numbers share no common substrate
C) Because numbers are indivisible
D) Because numbers are mental constructs

Key Takeaway
Quantity, unlike quality, has no opposite and cannot be intensified; differences are matters of measure, not degree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a kalam debate, a scholar argues that God's knowledge is 'more intense' than human knowledge. Using Razi's reasoning on quantity vs. quality, explain why this is a category error.
?
Identify whether 'length' and 'duration' are quantities or qualities. Then explain, using this lesson, why they have no opposites.

The Two Meanings of Infinity in Logic
Author defines infinity in real and metaphorical senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the real (ḥaqīqī) and metaphorical (majāzī) meanings of infinity as defined by Razi.

Core Idea
True infinity is the absence of a limit; metaphorical infinity is just a very large measure.

Razi defines infinity (al-lā nihāya) in two senses. The real sense means that for any portion taken, there is something beyond without needing to return. The metaphorical sense applies to distances that are merely large or difficult to traverse. This distinction is important in cosmological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of true infinity
Razi says true infinity is when something belongs to a thing but no end belongs to it. The key is that there is no actual extremity.
2
Understand the condition for true infinity
True infinity means that if you take any portion, you find something external without needing to return. This describes a non-cyclic infinite progression.
3
Distinguish the metaphorical usage
Metaphorical infinity is used for very large or difficult distances. It is not truly infinite but called infinite for convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Analyzing the Argument for the Existence of Place
Author argues for existence of place and refutes objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument for the existence of place (makan) in Islamic philosophy and evaluate the logical structure of the objections and refutations.

Core Idea
Place exists by necessity; arguments against it lead to absurdities or circularity.

In this passage, the author defends the reality of place as the inner surface of containing bodies. He anticipates objections by showing that denying place leads to logical contradictions, and he grounds his positive argument in the undeniable experience of motion. Follow along to see how the structure of premises and refutations builds a compelling case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of place
Locate the opening statement: place is the inner surface (al-sath al-batin) of the containing body. This is the doctrine being defended.
This definition avoids the concept of void, which is rejected in Aristotelian physics.
2
Outline the objector's division
The objector argues: if place exists, it must be either substance (jawhar) or accident (arad). Each branch is further divided and attacked. Note the exhaustive binary division.
This is a standard method of reducing possibilities to test them.
3
Analyze the refutations of each branch
For each branch, the author shows that it leads to an impossibility (interpenetration, circularity, or absurdity). Trace the logical chain: if extended substance → interpenetration; if non-extended substance → concession; if accident → change fixedness or circular dependency.
The refutations use reductio ad absurdum.
4
Extract the positive argument from necessity
The author counters all objections by invoking necessary knowledge: we observe motion from place to place, which would be impossible without real places. This is a direct appeal to intuition (wijdan) as evidence.
This shifts the burden of proof back to the objector.
5
Evaluate the overall argument
Assess whether the positive argument truly refutes the objections. Does the necessity claim hold? Consider whether the objector could accept the experience but reinterpret it.
This step trains critical engagement with philosophical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان المذهب المنصور في المشهورات أن المكان هو السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي للأجسام ذكرنا المكان حقا. في إثبات المكان من الناس من أنكره؛ لأنه لو كان موجودا لالأن إما أن يكون جوهرا أو عرضا. فإن كان جوهرا فإما أن يكون متحيزا أو لا يكون. والأول باطل بوجهين: أما الأول فلأن المحل حال في المحل فلو كان المكان محلا لمح أن المتمكن أيضا كذلك لزم تداخل الأجسام وهو محال؛ ولأنه لو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يجتمع العالم في مقدار حلة وسعال. وأما ثانيا فلأن كل متمكن فإنه يفتقر إلى الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، فإذا كل متمكن مكان، فلو كان المكان متمكنا لكان للمكان مكان، ومكانه إن كان هو الذي حصل فيه لزم كون كل واحد منهما مكانا للآخر وهو دور. وإن كان الثاني فقد سلم أنه المكان الكلي من غير تعيين ومر المطلوب. وأما إن كان عرضا فهو محال؛ لأنه إما أن يكون حالا في الممكن أو لا يكون. والأول باطل لوجهين: أما أولاً فلأن الحال ينتقل بانفعال محله والجسم المكان لا ينتقل باستقرار المتمكن. وأما ثانيا فلأن الحال محتاج إلى المحل والمحل الذي هو الجسم محتاج إلى الحين والمكان عندهم فلو كان المكان حالا لزم الدور. والثاني باطل لأنه لا يعقل الاختصاص. والجواب عن ذلك أن وجود المكان معلوم بالضرورة؛ فإنا نعلم بالوجدان أن المتحرك ينتقل من مكان إلى مكان، فلولا هذه الأمكنة لما صح ذلك. وإذا كان ذلك معلوما بالضرورة كان الاستدلال على نفيه باطلا.
Since the prevailing doctrine among the well-known [philosophers] is that place is the inner surface of the body containing bodies, we have mentioned place correctly. Regarding the proof of place, some people denied it; because if it existed, it would either be a substance or an accident. If it is a substance, then either it is extended or not. The first is invalid for two reasons: First, because the locus is in a locus, so if place were a locus, then the located would also be such, leading to interpenetration of bodies, which is impossible; and because if that were permissible, it would be permissible for the world to gather in a small measure. Second, because every located thing is in need of transfer from place to place, so every located thing has a place; if place were located, then place would have a place, and if its place is that in which it occurs, then each would be a place for the other, which is circular. If it is the second [i.e., not extended], then it is admitted that place is universal without specification, and the desired is conceded. If it is an accident, it is impossible; because either it inheres in a possible being or not. The first is invalid for two reasons: First, because the accident changes by the affection of its subject, while the body (place) does not change by the settling of the located. Second, because the accident needs a subject, and the subject (body) needs the located and place according to them; so if place were an accident, circularity would follow. The second is invalid because specificity is inconceivable. The answer to that is that the existence of place is known by necessity; for we know by intuition that the mover moves from place to place; were it not for these places, that would not be correct. And since that is known necessarily, the argument for its negation is invalid.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument for the existence of place (makan) and refutes objections. The author begins by stating the prevailing definition: place is the inner surface of the containing body. Then he addresses those who deny place. The argument proceeds by dividing the possibilities: if place exists, it must be either a substance or an accident. First, the substance option is split into extended (mutahayyiz) and non-extended. The extended substance is refuted because it leads to interpenetration of bodies and infinite regress. The non-extended substance leads to conceding the point. Second, the accident option is refuted because if place were an accident inhering in a body, it would change with the body's motion, but place is fixed; also it would cause circular dependency. Finally, the author gives a positive argument: the existence of place is known by necessity through the observed fact of motion from one location to another, so any argument denying it is invalid. The reasoning uses conditional syllogisms and reductio ad absurdum, typical of philosophical kalam.

Key Terms
المكان
السطح الباطن
الجوهر
العرض
المتحيز
الحال
المحل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Place must be a substance because it contains bodies.
B) We know place exists by necessity because we observe motion from place to place.
C) If place did not exist, bodies could not have accidents.
D) The definition of place as surface implies its existence.

Key Takeaway
Place is known by necessary intuition from motion; logical objections against it fail due to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar argument for the existence of time (zaman) using the same method: define time, then divide possible natures (substance/accident), refute each, and provide a positive argument from the experience of motion.
?
Identify the hidden premises in the objector's argument: what assumption does the objector make that leads to the absurdities? Hint: consider the assumption that place must be a locus (mahall).

Refuting Void and Defining Place & Directions
Argument against void and definitions of place and directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against void and distinguish the philosophical and common definitions of place and directions.

Core Idea
Void is impossible; place is either the containing surface or that which prevents falling.

This passage from al-Rāzī's Mulakhkhaṣ integrates a physical argument against the existence of void with a clear definition of place and the six directions. You will learn how the author uses logical reasoning and empirical evidence to deny void, and then how he defines 'place' differently for philosophers and common people, ultimately deriving directions from the finitude of dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target concept: void (khalā')
Find where the passage defines void as an empty distance devoid of body. Note that the author aims to refute its possibility.
Void is a central concept in natural philosophy; later mutakallimūn reject it absolutely.
2
Trace the refutation argument
The argument assumes that if void existed, collisions would not occur, so a thrown stone would continue until hitting the celestial sphere. The author then preempts an objection based on air mixed with void and dismisses it.
The logic is: if void → no resistance → infinite motion. Since resistance is observed, void must not exist.
3
Examine the empirical evidence
The passage lists examples like water rising in a sealed tube, a bottle crushing when air is removed, etc. These are presented as 'signs' that nature abhors void.
These are physical demonstrations used in falsafa to support the impossibility of void.
4
Distinguish two definitions of place (makān)
The author states that philosophers define place as the dimension or surface containing a body, while common people define it as that which prevents a thing from descending (e.g., earth as place for animals).
This distinction matters for later theological discussions about God and direction.
5
Derive the six directions (jihāt)
Because dimensions cannot extend infinitely, every straight line has two ends. For a body with three dimensions, we get six directions: above, below, right, left, front, back. The author also explains why a circle has no actual direction.
The derivation relies on the finitude of dimensions and the defining of direction as 'limit'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال الخلاء. فلو كانت المسافة خالية لما حصلت المصادمات فكان يجب أن لا تضعف تلك القوة فكان يجب أن لا يرجع المحجر إلا بعد وصوله إلى سطح الفلك. ولقائل أن يقول هذا إنما يلزم لو قلنا ليس بين السماء والأرض إلا الخلاء. فإن قال نقد ذلك؟ قلنا الغالب هو الهواء لكن من خلال خلا[؟] لا يلزم ذلك. وأيضاً فالشيء ذكر في الشفاء في الموضع الذي بين فيه أن من الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة سكوناً أن المتحرك القاسي كما يفيد المجذوب قوة تحركه إلى فوق فإنه يعيد قوة تكسر ذلك الموضع وكان هذا قوله فكيف يمكنه مع ذلك أن يقول إنه لولا حصالات الهواء لما رجع الجو؟ النهج الثاني العلامات. فقالوا إن الأنبوب القليل الضيق إذا كان أسفله ثقبة ضيقة لف[؟] أعلى فإذا فتح ذلك نزل الماء وإن ضم ما نزل وليس ذلك إلا لاتباع الخلاء. وكذا الأنبوبة إذا عين الحد طرفي الشفا[؟] الجواز في الماء وحمض الطرف الآخر صعد الماء مع أنه ليس من شأن الماء الصعود وذلك لأن الهواء ملائم لسطح الماء فإذا مص الهواء الجنب فتبعه الماتريج[؟]. وكذا الم[؟] يرتفع عند المحجة. وكذلك إذا أدخلنا داس[؟] ابنوبة داخل قارورة وأحكمنا الخلك الذي بين عنق القارورة وعنق الأنبوبة ليسد الخلك فإذا جزنا الأنبوبة والحالة هذه بحيث لا يدخل فيه الهواء انكرت القارورة إلى داخل وذلك لاستحالة الخلاء. وإن أدخلنا الأنبوبة إلى باطن القارورة أكثر انكسرت إلى الخارج وذلك لأن الأنبوبة كانت حلوة[؟] فإذا أدخلنا فيها الماء عملها فانشقب[؟] إلى خارج. ولأنه لو أمكن الجيل الجائع[؟] بعض المقادير أن يكون خالية فإذا أذنا بمشاهدة المار[؟] واعتمدنا عليها وجب أن يصعد الماء إليها من غير أن يمنع منها ولا تظهر البقابق والتفاخات. ولقائل أن يقول من الأحمد[؟] فإن كانت توهم عند الخلة الواحة[؟] تمام بنت[؟] بالبرهان القاطع أنها لا تعتمر وجهاً لسوى ذلك كان التعويل عليها المطالب القبلية منعاً من الضباع[؟] هذه المسألة. ثم قال: والشبهة بعد عنى ناقلة والميل إلى الإثبات بالحجة الأولى في نفسه لفظ العلماء أنا يطلقون لفظ المكان إما على البعد أو السطح الواقع المتوقع وعامة المشهور فإما أنا يطلقون على ما منع النت[؟] من النزول فيجعلون الأرض مكاناً للحيوان ولا يجعلون الهواء المحيط به مكاناً له حتى أنه لو وضعت اللقمة على رأس قبة بمقدار درهم لم يطلق لفظ المكان إلا على القدر الذي يمنعه من النزول. ثم قال: إن الجهة لما ثبت امتناع ذهاب الأبعاد إلى غير النهاية وجب أن يكون لكل بعد مستقيم نهايتان وافترضت له فيما بينه جهتان وطرفان والمشهورات للخط يمين وشمال وقدام وخلف وقوله في الخط صحيح أما السطح فإذا كان مربعا واعدت نهايته التي هي الخطوط كانت أربعا وإن أعبر جمعيها الطرف صارت ثمانية فإن كان مدورا ومتغايرا وغير ذلك من المضلعات فله حسب كل حد حد لأنه لا معنى للجهة إلا الطرف والدائرة لا جهة لها بالفعل وإنما بالقوة في هاتها عن متناهية إذ لا نقطة أولى بها من غيرها والحالة في الجسم كالحال في السطح له وسبب اشتهار هذه المقنعة ذاتي عامي وهو أن الإنسان يحيط به جنبان عليهما اليمين واليسار وظهر وبطن ورأس وقدمان فالجهة القوية التي منها ابتدأ الحركة سموها اليمين والآخر يقابلها والفرق في الإنسان ما يلي رأسه الأعلى والأسفل ما يلي رجله وفي الحيوانات الفوق ما يلي ظهورها والأسفل ما يلي بطونها والقدام ما يلي وجهها وكان بها الجاسة[؟] والإبصار والخلف ما يقابله والعالم يكون عندهم جهة سود[؟] هذه وهم على هذا القدب[؟] وهو أن الجسم من أن نفر فيه أبعاد ثلاثة لكل بعد ضفتان فله أطراف ستة.
Refutation of void. If the distance were empty, collisions would not occur, so it would be necessary that that force not weaken, so it would be necessary that the stone not return except after reaching the surface of the sphere. And it may be said: This only follows if we say there is nothing between the sky and the earth except void. If it is said: Refute that? We say: The dominant is air, but through void [?] that does not follow. Also, the thing mentioned in the Shifa in the place where he explained that among upward and downward motion there is rest: that the forcibly moved object, just as it gives the attracted body force to move upward, it returns force to break that place, and this was his statement. So how can he with that say that if not for the obstacles of air, the air would not return? The second approach: the signs. They said: The narrow tube if its bottom has a narrow hole and the top is sealed, if that is opened, water descends, and if it is closed, it does not descend, and that is only due to following void. Similarly, the tube if the limit of the two ends [??] in water and the other end is heated, water rises even though it is not in the nature of water to rise, because air is adherent to the surface of water, so if the air draws the side, the [??] follows it. Similarly, [??] rises at the [??]. Likewise, if we insert a tube inside a bottle and seal the gap between the neck of the bottle and the neck of the tube to close the gap, then if we bend the tube in this state such that no air enters it, the bottle is crushed inward, due to the impossibility of void. And if we insert the tube deeper into the bottle, it breaks outward, because the tube was [??] so if we insert water into it, it [??] outward. And if it were possible for the [??] some quantities to be empty, then if we allow [??] and rely on it, water would have to rise to it without any hindrance, and bubbles and [??] would not appear. And one may say: [??] if it is imagined at [??] completely established by decisive proof that it does not [??] a face other than that, the reliance on it would be a prior demand preventing [??] this issue. Then he said: And the doubt after [??] and the inclination towards proof by the first argument itself: the term of the scholars: they apply the term 'place' either to the dimension or the surface that is expected, and the common people apply it to what prevents [??] from descending, so they make the earth a place for animals and do not make the surrounding air a place for it, such that if a morsel is placed on top of a dome of about a dirham, the term 'place' is only applied to the amount that prevents it from descending. Then he said: Since it has been established that dimensions cannot go on infinitely, it is necessary that every straight line have two ends, and it is assumed between them two directions and two sides. The well-known for a line are right, left, front, back. His statement about a line is correct. As for a surface, if it is square, its ends, which are lines, are four, and if you combine them, the sides become eight. If it is round, or of other polygons, it has according to each limit a limit, because direction means nothing but the limit. A circle has no direction actually, but only potentially in [??] infinite, because no point is more worthy than another. The situation for a body is like the situation for a surface. The reason for the fame of this [??] is a common innate [??]: that a human has two sides: right and left, back and belly, head and feet. So the strong direction from which movement begins they call right, and the other opposite. The difference in humans: what is near the head is above, and below is what is near the feet. In animals, above is what is near the back, below is what is near the belly, front is what is near the face, and there are the sense of hearing and sight, and back is its opposite. The world according to them has a direction [??] of these, and they are upon this [??], which is that a body has three dimensions, each dimension has two sides, so it has six ends.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical discussion combining a refutation of the existence of void (khalā') with a definition of place (makān) and directions (jihāt). It begins with an argument from the impossibility of collision in a void: if space were empty, collisions could not occur, so a thrown stone would not return until hitting the celestial sphere. The author anticipates an objection based on assuming a mixture of air and void, then rejects it. He then presents empirical evidence, such as water rising in a tube due to suction, to demonstrate that nature abhors a void. The latter part defines place as either the encompassing surface (as per Aristotle) or the common understanding as that which prevents motion downward. Finally, the passage explains the six directions: since dimensions are finite, each straight line has two ends, and for a right line these are right, left, front, back; for a square surface there are four ends (lines) and eight sides; for a circle, no actual direction. For a body with three dimensions, there are six ends: above, below, right, left, front, back. The confusion between common and philosophical definitions is noted.

Void is impossible because it would eliminate resistance and make motion incoherent; place can be defined either as a containing surface (philosophical) or as a support that prevents falling (common), and the six directions derive from the three dimensions each having two ends.

Defining the Highest Genera via Incomplete Descriptions
Author argues that highest genera can only be described by incomplete descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a concept can only be defined by an incomplete description (rasm) rather than a complete definition (hadd), and explain why highest genera require rasm.

Core Idea
Highest genera have no higher genus, so only rasm is possible.

This passage from al-Razi addresses a fundamental limit of definition: some concepts, the highest categories like substance and quality, cannot be captured by a complete definition (hadd). Instead, logicians must rely on incomplete descriptions (rasm) that use negative or relational attributes. Understanding this exception prevents errors in defining the most general terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
In this passage, the terms that cannot be defined completely are the 'highest genera' (al-ajnas al-aliyah), such as quality (kayf). Note that these are the most general categories.
2
Recognize why a complete definition fails
A complete definition (hadd) requires a genus and differentia. The highest genera have no higher genus above them, so they cannot be defined by hadd.
This is the key logical point.
3
Distinguish between hadd and rasm
A hadd gives the essence (mahiyya) through genus and differentia, while a rasm describes the thing through essential accidents, negative attributes, or relations.
Rasm is an incomplete description.
4
Identify the type of rasm used
The passage mentions that the incomplete description can be negative (e.g., 'quality is not substance') or relative (e.g., 'quality is that which is predicated of something').
5
Verify that the description terms are more known
The author notes that the terms used in the description (like 'substance', 'quantity') must be more known (a'raf) than the defined term. Check if this condition holds.
This ensures the description clarifies rather than obscures.

The issues in it are difficulties which are not valid because it is not by essence indivisible, but through the unity of the known and complete knowledge. The highest genera have no definitions nor complete descriptions, but can only be defined by incomplete descriptions. Then the mentioned incomplete description is sometimes negative and sometimes relative. And those things must be more known than the defined. Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the difficulty of defining the highest genera (al-ajnas al-aliyah) such as substance, quantity, quality, etc. These are the most general categories under which everything falls. They cannot be defined by a complete definition (hadd tamm) because they have no higher genus to serve as the genus in the definition. Instead, they can only be described by incomplete descriptions (rasm naqis), which use negative attributes (e.g., quality is not substance) or relational attributes (e.g., quality is that which is predicated of something). The author notes that the terms used in the definition must be more known (a'raf) than the defined term. However, the highest genera are themselves the most known in conception but not in essence. The passage then gives an example: if one tries to define quality, one says it is not substance, not quantity, not relation, etc. This negative definition is valid as a rasm naqis. The text is garbled but the core point is that the highest categories are indefinable by hadd and rely on rasm.

Analyzing and Critiquing Definitions of Moisture
Author defines moisture and critiques two definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in a definition and evaluate whether a counterexample refutes it.

Core Idea
A definition must include all instances and exclude all non-instances; a single counterexample suffices to reject it.

In this passage, the author presents two possible definitions of moisture (al-rutūba) and then critiques them. You will learn how to analyze definitions by identifying their genus (the broader category) and differentia (the distinguishing feature), and then test them against counterexamples. This method is crucial in Islamic logic and fiqh for ensuring definitions are precise and comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus in each definition
Both definitions place moisture under the category of quality (kayfiyya). Note that the genus is the same, but the differentia differ.
The genus provides the broader class; the differentia narrows it down.
2
Extract the differentia for the first definition
The first definition's differentia is 'being free from internal bonds and easy to adhere and separate.' This is the specific characteristic that supposedly makes something moist.
3
Test the first definition with a counterexample
The author reports the Shaykh's objection: if adhesion is due to moisture, then a moister body should adhere more strongly. But water is very moist yet does not adhere like glue. This counterexample shows the differentia fails.
The objection uses a conditional syllogism (if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P).
4
Extract and test the second definition
The second definition's differentia is 'easy to take the shape of the container and easy to leave it.' The author hints that earth (turb) can take shape, yet is not considered moist, so this definition would wrongly include earth.
5
Conclude that both definitions are defective
Both definitions are rejected because they either include non-moist things or exclude genuinely moist things. This shows the need for a precise definition meeting the logical criteria.
The passage does not provide the correct definition; it only critiques two flawed ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يدل على ان الحارة النارية مخالفة بالنوع للحرارة الشمسية تاثيرهما في العين؟؟؟ وتفاوت في الرطوبة. والرطوبة هي الكيفية التي يكون بها الجسم خالياً من مرابط الشهية سهل الالتصاق بالغير سهل الانفصال عنه. وثانيها الكيفية التي يكون سهل التشكل بشكل الحاوي الغريب سهل الترك له. فإن جعلنا الرطوبة اسماً للأول لم يكن الهواء أطيب، وإن جعلناها اسماً للثاني كان الترب؟؟؟ لكن الشيخ أبطل الأول بأن الالتصاق بالغير لو كان لأجل الرطوبة لكان الأرطب أشد التصاقاً، لكن الثاني باطل.
And what indicates that the heat of fire is different in kind from solar heat is their effect on the eye... and variation in moisture. Moisture is the quality by which a body is free from bonds of appetite, easy to adhere to another, easy to separate from it. The second [definition] is the quality that is easy to take the form of the containing vessel, easy to leave it. If we make moisture a name for the first, then air is not moister; if we make it a name for the second, then dust is... But the Shaykh invalidated the first because if adhesion to another were due to moisture, then the moister [body] would be more adhering, but the second is false.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines the quality of moisture (al-rutūba) and critically examines two potential definitions. The author begins by noting that the heat of fire differs in kind from solar heat, as shown by their effects and by variation in moisture. Then two definitions of moisture are presented: (1) a quality that makes a body free from internal cohesion, easily adhering to another and easily separating; (2) a quality enabling a body to easily take the shape of its container and easily leave it. The author observes a terminological dispute: if 'moisture' is taken as the first definition, then air would not be considered moist (since air does not adhere); if taken as the second, then earth (turb) would be moist (since it can take shape). Then the author reports that the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sīnā) refuted the first definition: if adhesion were caused by moisture, then the moister a body is, the more it should adhere, but this is not true (e.g., water is moist but does not adhere as strongly as glue). The second definition is also rejected. This is a critical refinement of definitions typical of philosophical discussion on categories of quality.

Defining Moisture: Distinguishing Quality from Effect
Author defines moisture and defends definition against an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a quality and its effect in definitions, and refute objections that conflate the two.

Core Idea
Moisture is not adhesion but the disposition for it; conflating the two leads to false conclusions.

In this passage, the author defines moisture (al-rutūba) as a quality that disposes a body to easy adhesion and easy separation. He then anticipates an objection: if moisture causes adhesion, then honey, which adheres longer, would be moister than water—yet we know water is moister. The author's response reveals a crucial logical principle: a cause should not be confused with its effect. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how precise definitions protect against fallacious inferences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Locate the exact wording where the author defines moisture. Note the key phrase: 'the quality by which the body is disposed (yasta'iddu) to easy adhesion'. This is the definition.
The definition uses the concept of isti'dad (disposition/preparedness), not actual adhesion.
2
Analyze the objection's logical structure
The objection is a conditional syllogism: If adhesion is due to moisture, then the more enduring in adhesion is moister. Honey is more enduring in adhesion. Therefore honey is moister. Identify the premises and conclusion.
This is a modus ponens form: if P then Q, P, therefore Q. The form is valid, but the major premise is false.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
The author does not deny that honey adheres longer. Instead, he rejects the major premise: adhesion is not identical to moisture. Moisture is the disposition (isti'dad) for easy adhesion, not the actual adhesion. Therefore, a substance can be more adhesive due to other qualities (like viscosity) without being moister.
This is a distinction between a power (quwwa) and its act (fi'l).
4
Apply the analogy to reinforce the point
The author strengthens his case with an analogy: moisture is also defined as the disposition to easily receive shapes. Yet clay, which easily holds shapes, is not moister than water. This shows that the definition of moisture as a disposition remains consistent across different effects.
The analogy provides a parallel case that supports the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال لوكان الالتصاق بحسب الرطوبة لزم أن يكون الأَدْوَمُ التصاقاً أَرْطَبَ والعسل أَدْوَمُ التصاقاً فيكون أَرْطَبَ. فنقول: إنَّا لا نَجْعَلُ الرطوبة نفسَ الالتصاق بل الكيفية التي باعتبارها يَسْتَعِدُّ الجسم لسهولة الالتصاق، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون الأَدْوَمُ التصاقاً أَرْطَبَ. كما أنه لما جُعِلَت الرطوبة في الكيفية التي باعتبارها يَسْتَعِدُّ الجسم لسهولة قبول الأشكال، لم يلزم أن يكون الأَشَدُّ قبولاً للأشكال أَرْطَبَ.
It cannot be said: If adhesion were due to moisture, then the more enduring in adhesion would be moister, and honey is more enduring in adhesion, so it would be moister. We say: We do not make moisture the same as adhesion, but rather the quality by which the body is disposed to easy adhesion. And it does not follow from that that the more enduring in adhesion is moister. Just as when moisture was made the quality by which the body is disposed to easy reception of shapes, it did not follow that the one which receives shapes more strongly is moister.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a definitional clarification of moisture (rutuba) and defends it against a potential objection. The author first states the objection: if moisture is defined as the cause of easy adhesion, then honey, which adheres more persistently than water, would be moister—but experience shows water is moister. The objection uses a conditional syllogism: if adhesion is by moisture, then the more adhesive is moister; honey is more adhesive; so honey would be moister (which is false). The author refutes this by distinguishing between the quality itself and its effect. Moisture is not the act of adhesion but the disposition (isti'dad) of a body to adhere easily. Therefore, a substance can be more adhesive without being moister if its adhesion is due to another quality (e.g., viscosity). The author draws an analogy: moisture is also defined as the quality enabling easy reception of shapes (qubul al-ashkal). Just as being more shapeable does not make a body moister (clay is more shapeable than water but is drier), so being more adhesive does not make a body moister. This preserves the definition of moisture as a disposition, not an action. The passage demonstrates careful logical reasoning to avoid conflating the cause with the effect.

Key Takeaway
A quality should be defined by its disposition, not by its actual effect; conflating the two leads to false conclusions.

Defining Wetness and Dryness: Critique of Definitions in Physics
Author critiques earlier definitions and establishes his own definition of wetness and dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the author's definition of wet and dry, identify his logical critiques of alternative definitions, and apply his method to other natural qualities.

Core Idea
True definitions rely on essential criteria; wetness is not a sensible quality but a privation of obstacle.

The author begins by stating his own definition of wet and dry, then critically examines Ibn Sina's definition and the ontological status of wetness. This lesson walks through the logical method of definition and critique used in Islamic natural philosophy, showing how careful analysis resolves disputes about the nature of qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the author's definition: 'wet is that which has no obstacle to adhesion and separation' and the Shaykh's: 'that which has no obstacle to accepting shapes'. Notice the different differentia.
The author claims his definition is the truth (al-tahqiq).
2
Extract genus and differentia
Both definitions share the genus: 'that which has no obstacle' (or 'that which has an obstacle' for dry). The differentia is either 'adhesion and separation' or 'accepting shapes'. Consider which is more fundamental.
In logic, a definition is complete only if it includes essential attributes.
3
Follow the argument against wetness as a positive quality
The author argues that if wetness were a positive existential quality (like heat), it would be sensible. But we do not perceive wetness in air by sensation; we only know air is wet by proof. Therefore, wetness is not a sensible quality.
This is an empirical argument based on the absence of direct perception.
4
Understand the conclusion on the nature of wetness
The author concludes that wetness is either a privation (absence of obstacle) or a non-sensible existential quality. He then discusses classification: whether wetness is a genus with species or one species with mixtures.
Both possibilities are considered possible (mumkin) in the text.
5
Evaluate the critique of the Shaykh's definition
The author's critique focuses on the differentia. By preferring adhesion/separation over shape acceptance, he emphasizes behavior over form. Consider why this distinction matters for explaining phenomena like air wetness.
The passage hints that the Shaykh's definition may lead to incorrect classification.

Chapter of relation and wetness and dryness. From it, the truth is that the wet is that which has no obstacle in adhesion and separation according to what we said first, and no obstacle in accepting shapes according to what the Shaykh said, and the dry is that which has an obstacle. As for the spread of the Shaykh, it is that the opposition between them is by privation and possession, because wetness is either that interpretation of that receptivity and its acquisition. If it is [something] ... then the second is false. The body in itself is receptive to burning, so reception is obtained, and if the receptivity is of the body itself, then explaining it by something additional is invalid because one judgment cannot be both by essence and by other. It is established that wetness according to that interpretation must not be existence, and also if it is considered existential, it is not sensible at all, otherwise we would perceive it in air ... like cold and heat. If it were so, we would know by sensation that air is wet, but we do not know that except by proof, so we know this quality is not sensible. As for its interpretation, it is more likely that it is an existential sensible quality. Wetness is a genus having species. Some say that the wetness of water is different from the wetness of dates and different from the wetness of saliva, so wetness has species under it. Some make it one species and say the difference is due to mixing of dry with wet. Both sayings are possible regarding the judgment of wetness and dryness. As for their opposites, their togetherness is known necessarily, but whether there is a third like heat with black and white is doubtful. And if it exists, it is unknown. Why not make roughness the opposite of wetness and smoothness of dryness? We will mention later, God willing, that roughness and smoothness are among the qualities of touch.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a critical definitional discussion of wetness (rutuba) and dryness (yubusa). The author begins by stating his own definition: wet is that which has no obstacle to adhesion and separation, and dry is that which has an obstacle. He notes that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) defined wet in terms of accepting shapes easily. The author then examines whether the opposition between wet and dry is one of privation and possession, as if wetness is a positive quality and dryness its absence, or vice versa. He argues that if wetness is interpreted as a receptive quality, it cannot be an additional existential attribute because the body itself is already receptive to forms; explaining it by something extra would lead to infinite regress. Furthermore, if wetness were a positive sensible quality, we would perceive it in air, but we only know air's wetness by proof, so it is not directly sensible. On the alternative interpretation that wetness is a positive existential quality, it would be a genus with species, like the wetness of water, dates, and saliva differing. Some scholars hold it is one species with variations due to mixture with dry. The author then shifts to consider the opposites of wet and dry, stating that their simultaneous existence is known necessarily, but whether a third intermediate exists (like lukewarm between hot and cold) is doubtful. He questions why roughness and smoothness are not taken as opposites, but postpones that discussion. The passage illustrates the logical method of defining by genus and differentia and critiquing alternative definitions through arguments about existence, sensibility, and classification.
